RATING: 9.5
Worker placement, character ability selection, and tile-laying and a Wild West theme come together to make a solid if not overly original gaming experience.

Yet another worker placement game.  By this point, this would normally be a way overdone idea.  What saves this game from mediocrity is it's great theme (I've been waiting for a Wild West theme gamers game for a long time) and the different take on the worker placement mechanic.  The game lasts four rounds (and it seems both arbitrary and overly short, but I guess that helps keep it somewhat fresh) and the goal is to have the most points by game end.  It has a somewhat engine feel to it, as points generally (but not always) scale rapidly upwards towards the end of the game.

Each round, you select a character that has a special ability, a turn order, and a money holding limit.  The characters vary quite a bit in abilities and generally each have opportunites to be very good in certain situations and/or rounds.  You go into each round with some money that you start with and then that you carry over to the next round (here is where your money holding capacity is very important), and then you take worker placement actions in turn order.  There is path that is resolved in order, very similar to Caylus, and these options range from income generating spaces (which aggravatingly occur usually AFTER you really need the money), building purchasing spaces (a key use of money, and an important way to generate future income and points), point generating spaces (generally quite contested), and a few other situational spaces (such as road building, extra guns, etc.).  You also have the option to place your worker out in the town or wilderness of Carson City to purchase plots of land or to rob people's buildings of income.

The twist on the worker placement genre is that you can contest another player's placement.  This means that you are going to duel the other player, with the winner being the one with the greater firepower.  There are two ways to play this: 1) semi-random dice rolling, and 2) deterministic gun tiles.  I prefer option 2 but it's good to have the choice.  Generally you want to win a space, however there is some consolation in losing in that you get your worker back and therefore will have an extra placement the next round (the one problem is that there is NO compensation for the very last round).  There are several ways to acquire guns, there are several ways to aquire income, and there are several ways to score points (guns, plots of land, buildings, purchasing points with income, etc.).  

At the end of a round, you can only carry over so much income (based on your character) and any excess money is burned at a poor income to points ratio.  The theme (characters, gunfights, developing the town of Carson City, the buildings) really fit the theme well.  The game is a bit confrontational, but it is fun.  There SEEM to be several paths to victory, although guns generally are pretty overpowering.  Overall, this a relatively refreshing take on the tired worker placement mechanic, and it's a very solid gamer's game, with classic euro mechanics.  Looking forward to Xavier Georges next offering.

Own the [boardgame=129508]Gold & Guns[/boardgame], [boardgame=177353]Horses & Heroes[/boardgame],and [boardgame=83538]A New Beginning[/boardgame] expansions.

2016-03-02
8->7
A couple of more plays have shoved the true worth of Carson City. I find it a good and well designed game. While a little bookkeeping still necessary, administrative tasks are minimised to great extent. Hence you can really concentrate on playing.

Interaction between players heavier than in regular worker placements. Personally I like it.

Despite being well designed the Carson City lacks that certain elegance, and I think it is caused by too many layers upon each other. Basically, you are building an engine to generate resources. This is standard stuff. However, the part that dims the brightest shine of the game is that you actually have to take separate actions to score your engine or to buy victory points with resources. Does not sound like a big deal, but when you have a limited amount of time and actions, I'd much prefer to concentrate on the fun part of building and producing instead of trying to remember to score points at every possible occasion. For some reason this makes the game feel a bit slow.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very good worker placement game with an interesting mix of mechanics. It has a number of similarities to Caylus, but the gunfight aspect really sets it apart from that game. Unlike the typical worker placement game where the first person choosing an action gets it, Carson City allows players to fight over action spaces. The dice aspect adds a random factor, but it's one that doesn't seem to have a disproportionate effect on the game. There are ways to mitigate the luck by having more guns than your opponents or by avoiding spaces where a fight is likely to occur, and losing a fight isn't overly detrimental to a player since that player gets their worker back, which both helps them win future fights on that turn and also allows them an additional worker the next turn.

The role selection aspect of the game also creates interesting decisions that will shape a player's strategy.

The ability to rob other players' buildings wasn't used at all in my first two plays (though there were threats of it in the second), and it's an option that can easily be ignored if the players in the game don't care for that kind of confrontation.  In a 3+ player game, this option is probably a low-efficiency play, but I could see it being situationally useful if one player creates an ultra-profitable board setup that needs to be reined in.

Thematically, the game does have an Old West vibe to it.  Yes, many of the mechanisms are contrived and don't make much sense thematically, but there is enough theme in the game to make it interesting.

RATING: 7.7

As the game hots up, the competition gets quite rife and the duels are plenty. My one minor doubt at the moment is in the power of the last "exchange 5 dollars for points" in the last round - having won both times by building towards an unbeatable total of guns by the last round. Not yet a serious doubt as more play is needed - excellent game.

RATING: 7
A mildly interesting role selection/worker placement game.  Decent number of choices in any given round, but I feel like a lot of them lead to the same place.  Not a ton of differentiation except in the buildings a player focuses on.

Primarily, you're building an engine.  Buildings --> $ --> VP.  There are a few other ways to get $ and VP, but they're once-per-round and likely to earn you far less.

Dueling adds a nice touch of player interaction.  You cannot ignore firepower, or else you will likely be stomped.  Repeatedly.  Unless you have a Church.  And even then, that just protects your buildings.  No, firepower can be pretty important.

Overall, fun but it's missing something.  I feel like there isn't a solid overarching thread to the game - no beginning, middle and end, just an incremental creep to oh-crap-how-do-I-get-VPs-again.  I'll definitely play this again, but it's not a must for me.

RATING: 9.5
Carson City combines city building, role selection, worker placement, and tightly-themed player interaction (cowboy dueling) and comes out a big winner.

Players select a role each turn (Sheriff, Grocer, Settler, Mercenery, etc.) and then alternate turns in placing their cowboys on the board (cowboy meeples!). Players have very tough choices before them: do I place my cowboy on a promising parcel of land so that I can build on it? Do I buy a saloon for a cheap price, hoping no other players duel me for it, or do I pay a little more to make sure it remains uncontested? Do I pick the Sheriff this turn and secure my actions from being taken in a duel, or increase my firepower with the Mercenary? Do I risk fighting another player for the right to build a certain building? Do I dare roll the dice and gamble for extra income? So many choices, so much fun.

QWG's production values--always top notch--are yet again first rate. The artwork is evocative and the pieces are all extremely durable, functional, and thematically charged. There is also a genuine pleasure in watching Carson City grow from a tiny house into a full-fledged Western town. Kudos to QWG for making another game that makes you really WANT to play with those pieces!

As a highly involved strategy game, Carson City is demanding--especially on the first play--but it is worth it! Although the rules are sometimes ambiguous and in a few cases difficult to follow, the notes on BGG offer welcome clarifications.

In short: I found Carson City utterly engaging and atmospheric, with lots of room for surprise and tension in player dueling, and makes for a welcome breath of fresh air overall. Very highly recommended!

RATING: 9.6
I have utterly no interest in wild west themed games; yet I'm madly in love with Carson City, which is thoroughly steeped in the cowboy theme! In fact, in terms of brain burners (which Carson City most definitely is!), it's one of the most well themed advanced strategy games I've yet played. Sure, it can be pretty cutthroat....but that is totally up to how you and your opponents choose to play: gunfights are NOT mandatory (though certainly advisable at times!). And loosing a duel (except in the final turn) does have its benefits! 
A fantastic game, but you may want to be careful with whom you play it; with the right (or wrong?) crowd of eurogamers, it can degenerate into an AP lovefest. Now, if only M.Georges will retheme it to something that appeals to me.....the North American fur trade perhaps? A viking settlement in the Hebrides?

RATING: 6
2-5(4)

I enjoy this a bit more than I thought I would. It integrates mechanisms from a lot of classics in a pretty nice way. Worker placement from Caylus, role selection like Citadels or Puerto Rico (though different from each), a spacial element, etc. There's also a good amount of variety and freshness in the plays.

The spacial element feels just sort of "there" ... there may be some challenge in placing a building (lack of roads or too expensive to get the plot), but often times placements are a foregone conclusion.

The roles are a nice twist, though. Each clearly directing that turn's strategy, with nice mechanisms for managing turn order and the money carryover (thus, choosing a weaker role this round may set up a big turn).

The worker placement element is really familiar, but done well, and engagingly, with early spaces setting up later ones. Money can be really tight and the gradually escalating point costs is a great risk-reward mechanism. Duels can also really add to the interaction and really change the way a game will play out (every duel results in a "half-wasted" turn at least ... half-wasted because they'll get their cowboy for next turn) and it does very well the mechanism of giving you a reason to pass early and save your cowboys (I feel Caylus, with the cost for placement after a pass, is clunkier).

Of the mid-weight worker placements, I like this way better than something like Egizia.

RATING: 8
An assemblage of familiar mechanics, including character selection and worker placement on a Caylus-style road.  What might have been another boring efficiency game has life injected by a fair degree of player interaction through gunfights for contested spots and some vicious struggles over prime plots of land. I particularly like the this town ain't big enough for the five of us competitiveness of a full complement of players.

The net result is a nice thematic feel of rough and tumble competition in a rapidly growing Old West town.  This is helped by some quite good artwork.  Carson City packs a fair bit into a short playing time. Not bad at all.

RATING: 8.3
For us, this was one of those games that takes a while to "click".  Once you get it, though, it turns into a pretty good gem of a game, somewhat reminiscent of Caylus (which I'm a big fan of).  I think the rule book could be improved, thereby decreasing the learning curve associated with our first experience.  In many respects, gameplay just wasn't clear.  Like I said, you do eventally get it, and it is a neat game once that happens.

As a side note, I have had a terrible experience with Eagle Games.  My new, sealed copy had components that were very moldy.  I tried to contact Eagle Games numerous times, but never received a response.  What's worse, after researching the issue, I know that they've been aware of the issue for quite some time, and yet the game is still selling, and for full price at that (moldy components and all).  That is unacceptable in my book.

UPDATE (06/06/2013): Eagle Games did eventually contact me, and they sent me new meeples.  By that point, I had already cleaned all the old components, which took a lot of time.

RATING: 7
Seems heavily-influenced by a lesser-known founding father of worker placement games: Way Out West.  I find it fairly clunky (road/house placement, unintuitive player aid, etc.) and not particularly thematic.  I think the game engine is decent - if not a bit dependent on key duels - and I'm always happy to see integrated variants.  This one has a lot: an alternative side for each of seven character cards, an alternative game board with slightly modified rules, lower-luck duel resolution (which still doesn't reduce the criticality IMO), and even a real-time placement option.  I've played with all but real-time, and while I think the river board isn't a big deal and that the variant characters are generally worse, I'm still glad to have all the choice.  It makes me want to revisit Way Out West (traded away), or Caylus.

RATING: 10
I'm quite impressed. The theme is fun and fits well enough. The best feature of this game is, unlike many modern Euros and the worker placement games in particular, it has a common tile placement area instead of individual player-mats. That gives it an element of player interaction and 'themeiness' that other games in that genre tend to lack. The combination of worker placement and role selection seems a bit redundant, but it works.

Although it's not perfect. I'm not sure about the shooting mechanic; it seems like its too big a risk in game with not as many turns to . My first play had no fighting at all. Aside from that, it feels a bit too short at four times, but probably better to be too short than too long. Besides, it flows quite well.

I hope that I could find a copy of my own!

RATING: 8.1
A real surprise for me when I first played it in Essen 2009. I'm not really fond of the western theme but with this game it really works. In this game you are settling a town in the west. It is a nice worker placement game with a lot of direct player interaction. Something that is missing in so many games. There is quite a bit of luck involved but with the tile variant you can take most of it out if it bothers you. Your limited workers (cowboys) force you to make tough decisions. Not the least of these is to go for vp's early when they are cheap (but you won't have a lot of money), or go for them later and get a much worse exchange rate. All in all a well tested game which combines a lot of well known mechanics into a solid game.

Gameplay: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Fun/Social: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Components: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Rules: :star::star::star::star::halfstar:

RATING: 9
Likes:
- Very thematic worker placement game.
- A TON of interesting decisions to make each and every turn
- Playing time is perfect.  Will take longer during first play, but can move very quickly once everyone knows how to play.
- One of the best worker placement games out there.  This one will hold up well and be around in 5 - 10 years.

Dislikes:
- Tough game to explain.  New players are going to struggle to understand until probably half way through the game
- Other players choices can GREATLY mess up your masterpiece plan
- The dueling tokens are probably not my style, would just prefer dice rolls, but the majority of players are going to like these at it makes the game less random.

Notes:
The 1st edition of this game has meh components.  I picked up a used copy of the 1st edition because of the price, however if getting a new copy, 2nd edition is the way to go.

RATING: 6
Won as a door prize at the 2010 Boardgaming for Diabetes fundraiser.  Thanks to Mike & Waleah for hosting, and Drexoll Games for donating.

I heard it described as a more-complex Oregon.  I can see the connection -- they both have wild-west themes with Carcassonne people with big ten-gallon hats, and you score points by having your people near buildings and buildings near people.

I can objectively describe it as a "good game" without feeling at all the need to keep it.  I prefer playing duels with dice, but then the dice pissed off the deep strategists who complained about the luck.  It's called "drama", and you can still strategise to minimise the luck.  Whatever, I don't want to own a complex game that will cause complex gamers to grumble.

RATING: 6
I really enjoy this.  Love the theme and the game reminds me of Caylus and Oregon (at least how the board forms and how it pays out).  The duels can be rough and really take you out of the game if you get robbed but it works with the theme and you just need to be aware.  The first game took us about 2.5 hours with five but I would expect that time to drop some as we know how to play now.  It appears to be a lot of ways to go about winning, as well.  It sucked me in without taxing me like some games can.  I'm also a sucker for city games and this is one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Carson City as we have it is pretty good.  However, I can't help but think it could be so much better.  In particular I like the land parcel system that seems to hint at trying to buy outlying land cheap early rather than the expensive stuff in the city core.  A speculation game like that would be fantastic.  As it is, the game only lasts four rounds and money is abundant enough that this strategy is worthless.

The game is your typical worker placement with the twist that you can attack people and take their action.  I have heard opposing viewpoints on this mechanism.  Most of the time losing just delays your action one turn, which is not enough for some people.  For others it is devastating.  I lean towards the latter, particularly in the last round where you don't get another action and the point spaces can make or break an entire game.  

In fact I dislike the whole money situation.  Limiting carryover of money between rounds and slowly excluding the money to points spaces creates all kinds of awkward situations, particularly in the last round.  It often goes to whoever takes the Sherriff or the guy with the most guns and it can totally wreck a game.

However, the game is fast and pretty fun and I can always sit there thinking about an awesome land speculation game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
From the theme onward game annoyed me.  Amount of direct conflict makes it impossible to formulate consistent strategies without constant fear of very-hard-to-predict interruptions. Later also leads to very high likelihood of grudge matches and meta-game influenced play.

Key to playing it appears to be some sort of mixture between bullying and appeasement. While this is something I am willing to tolerate in a light game such as Small World - in a game with lots of strategic dept it is very distracting and potentially frustrating. 

I do like some new things that Carson city brings to the table but I am almost certain never to play it again.

RATING: 7
Rating based on one play. Pretty clean rules, a clear layout of action order and a euro that's actually fairly thematic! This is a game you definitely need to play a few times to estimate the balance of decisions. Novice players will get their butt kicked for certain. There's the standard fare of money and victory points, but scores will differentiate more than some other euros.

Like most worker placements there are all kinds of little dillemas but with Carson City workers can actually battle each other. Battle is decided by a single D6 roll, but you can acquire firepower to increase the odds of winning. Still, this sometimes leads to 50/50 win or lose chances and since the game only lasts four rounds, that can have a huge impact, losing all your money you wanted to convert to victory points. The swingy nature of this game when rolling a 1 losing to the other player's rolling 6 will certainly turn some players away.

It's a pity the few fiddly parts of the rules are not helped much by a lacking player reference. That explains the many homemade versions that are available in the files section.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
My initial rating of this is 8.5.  I like the implementation of the worker placement/auction mechanisms, and I quite like that you have an option for duelling over the squares.  It opens the game up somewhat, and you can finally do something about that player that always seems to be in your way!  Adding the ability to rob buildings in the town is also a nice addition.  Players need to be careful that they do not fall too far behind on their meeple count or risk having lucrative buildings robbed.  The buildings are well conceived, as some (ranches and mines) want you to build in areas with little development and others want to be in the main town.  The ability to buy parcels and prevent your opponents from benefitting from them is great, and I find that trying to buy/control some buildings really start wars amongst the players (particularly the jail for the extra revolvers and the church for the ability to protect adjacent buildings).  If all this was not enough you get a role selection mechanic and a fun theme to boot!

I'm looking forward to getting this to the table again soon, and trying the "advanced" versions of the roles.

As much as I liked this it did not get as warm a reception from any of the other people I played it with.  Sadly I sold it to make space for more played games in my collection.

RATING: 9
One of the best games from Essen 2009.

Yes, it is another worker placement game with a very famililar Caylus aspect of a road of actions that is resolved in order. But that's where the similarities end. The gameplay feels nothing like Caylus.

The conflict (duels with guns!) and scoring is interesting and refreshens a tried mechanic. (The game does not offer any new mechanics - it's role selection and worker placement). The game is highly thematic and it feels like you're settling the wild west and resolving conflicts by sending opponents' cowboys to the hospital/morgue by gun fights. Luv the nasty in this game. It's a confrontational in-your-face euro at its best.

Ignore the "duels are too random" criticisms. You can play with the tile variant (tiles should have been the standard game with dice as the variant) which pretty well eliminates most of the luck elements. I have won the game with one gun as my fire power. (ie losing every duel). 

Most of the other criticisms of this game are from pansies who can't take a bloody nose because they lost actions from losing gun fights. This game is not for those who show up at the O.K. Corral with forks as weapons. Besides, if you can't fight, the game offers lots of other balanced ways of achieving VPs. Sheesh.

Own the Master Print edition with "the Indian" expansion.

4 players seem to be the sweet spot now that I've played a 2, 3 and 4 player game. The amount of depth and replayability is amazing for its short playing time. (1hr 45m with my AP group; otherwise 1hr 15min).

Update: initial Essen impression of 7.5 has been increased to 9. After 11 games, this baby still has legs. I enjoy both the tile and dice versions of this game.

**COME W' GUNS ABLAZIN' CUZ NO GUNS, NO GLORY**

Update Nov 2009 to Nov 2012: after 3 years and 20 games, this baby still has legs.

RATING: 7
Upon hearing preliminary details of this one, it was at the top of my Essen wishlist - but, like many games, once reports of the 2er started to trickle in, I was somewhat dismayed.  Even Xavier George wrote a blurb in the forums, which read to me as an apologia for what was to be a sub-par experience.  Too much open space, too little competition, and too much focus on making money.

Well, some of that is true, but the good new is, it doesn't matter.  What is true is that making money is probably the dominant strategy - whereas in the 4 or 5er there is so much competition that taking houses, plots, or guns could be a VP strategy on their own, this is not true for two.  If you aren't making money, you won't be in contention.  The good news is that there are many ways to make money, and all the other ways of generating VPs become sidelines that you can act on as contingencies dictate.  

There is no problem with too much open space in the 2er.  We've played both sides of the board, and on the regular (non-river) side, there is more space to maneuver, but that can come back to bite you in the ass (for instance, with houses being built in town being more open to opponent's use with more open terrain to build in).  On the regular board, ranches and mines become more valuable.  On the river side, the town is much smaller, more compact and cutthroat, which doesn't necessarily lead to more confrontation, but presses those sideline VPs into greater service.  Overall, there is plenty of confrontation and dueling in the 2er - you can avoid it at times, and at times you need to embrace it.  It makes the game much less chaotic and more strategic, and there is plenty of strategy to mull over and decisions to make in this one.  

This has surpassed what I hoped it would be.  Worker placement here feels genuinely fresh and thematic, and the multiple paths to victory, combined with all the variants and variables, makes this one replayable.  The games play out quick, adding to the addictive nature of this one.  I'd recommend playing with the red sides of the character cards after you get a flavor for the yellow, as the yellow sides are out-and-out unbalanced, and the rest of the game is pretty finely tuned, but the nice thing is, you can mix and match red and yellow sides to give the game the flavor you want.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
My favorite worker-placement game, and one of my all-time favorite Euros. It blends all of the interesting decisions presented by a great Euro game—role selection, careful timing of events, turn order considerations, area control, economic engine building, VP maximization, etc.—with the awesome addition of the duels. 

Being able to oust someone for a spot not only gives you many more options during your turn, but it also adds a level of risk and drama that rarely occurs in a Euro. It also adds a wonderful psychological element to the game, in which the mere threat of a duel can be just as powerful as the fight itself, and people will let you have your way if they know you carry the guns. It makes the game not only a very rich psychological experience, but also makes the theme emerge that much more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Carson City is one of my absolute favorite worker placement games. It shares the "road" of action spaces with Caylus, but adds a city building grid, and dice tossing (!!) for showdowns over spaces if players duel at the location.

So why do I like this game so much? First, the worker placement game in general is a favorite of mine, as it keeps everyone involved and adds that tension of choices of wanting to do three things and having to pick one. But what separates Carson City specifically from the increasing number of worker placement games is that it is incredibly thematic. Players work together on building a town on the board, and you get to plan on how much that ranch or bank will earn you as other buildings emerge on the board around it. The game requires a lot of planning regarding the synergy between buildings that are placed adjacent.

All this is done in a mere four turns--that's it. You need to plan to accomplish a lot in a little time. And I don't care what some people say, I love rolling dice to settle a duel. The theme is fantastic. 

I haven't even scratched the surface of trying some of the different variants in the box (the side of the board with the river, the gun tiles instead of dice, etc.). I do know, though, that I always want to play this game, and I need to give it more love.

Update: sold this in an auction only in anticipation of the kickstarter big box edition.

Carson City is a strategic game. The game is played in 4 rounds and in each one of them the players choose a character that gives certain advantages, after that cowboys are placed on action fields on the board to build Carson city. Players can claim ground, erect special buildings, houses or construct roads. When there are several players on 1 action field, a duel is fought. He who earns most points wins the game.

RATING: 7
Update: Turns out cowboys defeated in duels are returned to player's available cowboy supply, not general supply. Doh! Much better with correct rules!

Not super well received by my group, which is frustrating, because I liked it. Enough to play back to back. There are a few similar core mechanics here: worker placement, role selection, and tile laying. I was a bit concerned by the dueling aspect based on comments I read prior to playing, but in my two games, I don't think the dice ever decided a duel. The base value always won. And usually one player went very strong into guns, and when you get +3-6 relative to someone else, each player adding 1d6 doesn't really change much. I need to play more, but in both games it seemed that the player who tries to [i]catch[/i] the player with the most guns loses. Need to test that some more. Double sided role selection cards is nice, for variety. I [i]don't[/i] like the "strategic" variant for dueling where each player chooses a strength from a set of tiles, a la A Game of Thrones. I prefer luck of dice to luck of guessing what the other player will pick. Actually, as I'm typing this, I was just thinking that a good compromise might be to NOT lose your dueling token if you lose the duel. Hmmm... that might work. Need to try that. Dueling basically is the way to resolve when two or more players put workers on the same spot. The board play is fairly interesting because you have a Santiago-like situation where roads have to connect to (most) buildings. But then buildings because income sourecs, rather than just points at the end,  and the income is relative to positioning of variuous related buildings. As I type this, I feel like I want to play again, which is a good sign.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Carson City is a title I found hard going at first until I was explained the game, and was able to focus more on its structure. Although obvious now, what makes this game difficult to learn from the rules is that all actions are in what you can do on the action track and on the playing area. The basic game (placing cowboys, resolving them, selling off excess money) is really very simple in comparison. In fact, Carson City appears as a very generic economic engine where all tuning has been put in the actions themselves. The fact that you get a surprising number of variants along with the base game underlines this aspect. I'm not sure I like this very much: yes, it makes the game quite variable, but what exactly is the game then but an ensemble of programmable units which turned out to yield a playable result—one of several, adding insult to injury—of sorts? 

In any case, the game comes with two main variants. There are more (topological, and having less luck when duelling other players), but the two I'm most concerned with are the yellow and red characters. The yellow characters are those which are usually taught the game with. They feature rather large monetary safeguards, lots of guns, and lots of free actions. The result is that the game is fairly agressive. It pays off to be a crook by preying on other people's hard construction work. The mode of play thus invoked is not everyone's cup of tea. The red characters on the other hand force players to take a more balanced approach. There are less possibilities to get extra actions and guns; and more possibilities to obtain points. In other words, you do not duel for the heck of it, and use the cowboys you have in a more thoughtful way. This mode of play is so different from the one invoked by the yellow characters that it wouldn't be fair to award both variants the same rate. Worse, ideally you would want to play both before deciding to purchase this title. Always keep this in mind when you read someone's opinion on this game. [i]How did he play it?[/i]

The actual game is a bit of a chore, though. There is a lot of silly bookkeeping to take care of (especially in working out the current values of buildings as those change as their surroundings change) and although you do get the pleasure of viewing a city appear on the board, I find it at odds with the clean efficiency you need to emerge victoriously. There is a lot going on, but in the end it doesn't feel like an interesting challenge apart from being more efficient than your opponent. I guess you could say I feel somewhat indifferent towards this design: for something so diverse, the game itself is a bit bland. Does the game have redeeming qualities? Yes. The graphics are beautiful and you get a lot of game for a very fair price. There are tons of avenues to explore in getting the most out of your cash (or bullets), although the duelling in the basic version (which relies on a random factor) may cause a few disgruntled faces now and then. But as you would expect, there is a variant dealing with this particular issue. If you like a plethora of small tactical choices, Carson City is your game. If not, then it feels bloated and in need of a good pruning, even though it all seems to hold together quite well. 

[u]RED VARIANT[/u]: 6

[u]YELLOW VARIANT[/u]: 5

RATING: 6.75
Carson City is another worker placement game, although in this case you've got cowboys.  And if the spot you want is taken, you just put your man there and have a good ol' fashioned shoot-out!  Eventually you stake claims of land, cover those with buildings, and try to cash out for as many VPs as possible each round.

This game took far too long to explain (and it's in desperate need of better player aids), but then once the game gets going, it plays relatively quickly.  But that's only true if you don't have any AP-prone players!  That can make this drag needlessly.

The real frustration was not being able to appreciate how it all fit together and the rhythm of the game, so you understood how long you had to accomplish your goals. Duels felt forced, and the loser is then at a real disadvantage (One extra cowboy next round? Not a big deal.) That being said, I'm still feeling like I need to give this one another try.

RATING: 7
Components: Cool cowboy meeples. Money that's easy to use. Clear iconography. Good artwork. A few things unclear in the rules (especially regarding roads). I like how the buildings make it easy to keep track of their current worth.

Experience: Solid worker-placement. Every cowboy placement counts (including witholding a cowboy to help in duels), and almost every space is desirable. With 2 players, there were spaces that were never used, but I can see these making sense with more players.

I like the natural balance mechanism of the duels, whereby the loser of a duel will have one additional cowboy to help in subsequent duels, thus making it less likely that they'll lose again. Still, the capriciousness of the duels, especially when dueling over who gets the triple-guns, can really throw a wrench in a person's plans.

The money cap is interesting. If I take an action that allows me to really increase my income, I need to make sure I can also spend that income as well, otherwise I waste it at the 10:1 ratio. The worsening cash-in ratio and fast pace of the game also puts extra pressure on me to think ahead and time my actions just right.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Quizá lo mejor de Essen'09. Un jueguito divertido, cuatro turnos, en cada turno 3 fases, seleccionar un personaje que te ayudará durante el turno y que además sirve para decidir en qué orden jugarás el resto de fases y cuánto dinerito podrás ahorrar para el próximo turno. Una segunda fase de colocación de "vaqueros", al más puro estilo Caylus, sólo que se permite usar la misma casilla por dos jugadores, en cuyo caso habrá un duelo para ver quien consigue hacer la acción (esta es la salsita del juego). Una última fase en la que se van ejecutando en orden todas las casillas seleccionadas con los vaqueros de cada jugador. Junto con el tema de los duelos también es importante el detalle de la pasta que puedes llevarte de un turno a otro, hay que saber jugar bien el personaje que toca en cada turno, teniendo en cuenta no sólo la ayuda que proporciona, sino también la pasta que te permite ahorrar.

Es un juego sencillo, algo dado al análisis parálisis, sobre todo las primeras partidas, después te das cuenta que hay tantas posibilidades buenas que no hace falta pensarlo tanto. Perdona bastante los errores, y en general asegura un final de partida apretado. Tiene una parte "económica" en el sentido de que todo lo que haces es para ganar pasta, y la pasta la invertirás en puntos de victoria, bien directamente (comprándo los puntos) o indirectamente (comprando edificios/parcelas que después te darán puntos). Tiene también bastante interacción, principalmente a través de los duelos, y es interacción de la que hace pupita, puesto que normalmente interactúas para quitarle opciones al rival.

Un buen juego.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Very cool worker placement game. A lot of interesting twists... build mines next to mountains and ranches in the open spaces. Build in the city, but build in such a way that your buildings benefit you more than neighboring players.

There's a role selection at the start of every round which determines your special ability for that round, as well as your turn order.

If someone else wants your action space, there's a gunfight to determine the winner. The loser gets his worker back, but the winner gets to take the action.

This all works together wonderfully... it's much faster paced than Caylus (for example), and it's got a fully functional and very fun theme. Nice!

(I should mention that the rulebook is vague on some points and there was much head scratching on the first play... but the second play was great.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
[2009-11-25 Rating: n.a. -> 8.5]

Overview:
--------------
In Carson City 2 to 5 players participate in the development of the eponymous city in the North American Wild West. Over four rounds, the players try to expand their personal wealth by buying land, constructing buildings or robbing their opponents. This wealth can then be used to buy victory points. At the end of this worker placement game, the player with the most victory points wins.
Each of the four rounds consists of the following phases:

1. Choose a personality:
The game comes with seven personality cards which do have two effects: First, each one grants the players who selected it a unique bonus (like reduced building costs). Second, the turn order of the round is decided by the number that is printed on each card. So during your selection you have to evaluate which of these two aspects is more important to you in the current situation.

2. Cowboy Placement:
Each round the players receive a certain amount of cowboys. Until all players have passed, each player chooses one of the following options:
- Place a cowboy on an action square: The game board has an action track printed on it. The available action squares on this track allow you to buy a building, to get money for your possessions or to convert your money into victory points among other things. 
- Place a cowboy on a parcel: The map-part of the game is divided into parcels. You can place one of your cowboys into one in order to buy the parcel later. You need a parcel in order to build a building or to deny your opponents the benefits of this parcel.
- Attack or defend a building: You can place a cowboy on one of your building in order to defend it or place your cowboy onto an opponent’s building to attack it and rob some money later.
- Pass: You can choose to pass even if you still have cowboys left that you could use.

3. Resolve cowboy placement:
Once all players have passed, the action track is resolved in accordance with the sequence on the board. If more than one player has placed a cowboy on the same action square or parcel, a duel, which is resolved using a dice, will follow (in most cases). The winner gets to take the action or is allowed to buy the parcel, the loser gets his cowboy back for later usage. During this phase, new buildings are acquired. They will generate income and/or grant you some other benefits.

4. Generate income:
Buildings players own generate a variable income. If no foreign cowboy was placed on your building, you receive the complete amount. If one or more opponents placed a cowboy on your building, a duel follows to determine the attacker. If you didn’t place a defender on your building, the attacker gets half of the income of that building. Otherwise you duel the attacker and if you win, the attacker get’s nothing.

5. End of turn:
After phase 4 is resolved, some housekeeping has to be done (e.g. refresh acquired buildings) and the next round starts with phase 1.

Comments:
---------------
* After my first play of Carson City, I was really surprised how many cutthroat decisions you have to make during the four rounds. The game feels quite heavy and also a little overwhelming but in a good way. I didn’t expect that after reading the rules. While I like this, it might be too much for others.

* Carson City is a very open game. You seem to have a wide array of options to implement your own strategy with. Furthermore, I guess (or better: hope) there are also some nice building combos to discover. All in all it seems as if there are a lot of viable paths to victory and I’m looking forward to discover a lot of them.

* I like the addition of direct conflict through duels. It a nice change of pace from other worker placement games.

* In my opinion, this is a gamer’s game. There’s a lot going on you have to figure out and I guess a bad turn early can easily cost you the whole game. So it might not be that easy to find players for it.

* The theme and the whole experience of the game are great. I really feel like I’m building my own little part of the city and while I’m doing this, I’m constantly stressed because some of my fellows might turn into outlaws and rob me. Great fun!

RATING: 6
Players are investing in Carson City, out in the American West.  Four turn worker placement game, where players juggle cash, cowboys, plots, guns and assorted other stuff.  Parcels of land are picked up and developed with houses, mines, saloons, etc, which earn income, which can be reinvested into further infrastructure, or into victory points - which is what it's all about.  Busy game, very much in the Caylus fashion - almost a western version of Caylus, but with a much more interesting on board placement game, as the town is expanded.  I thought it worked quite well, but needs a game or three to get up to speed - there is a bit going on.  I have a slight concern that it is very dependent on owning guns, and even then bad die rolling can torpedo your game as you get ousted from VP spots you're banking on.  Played with a couple of rules mistakes fixed, and bumped it up to an 8.  I like this one ... quite mean, but fast and works well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Yep, “Caylus with guns” is an apt description and it gives CC its unique flavor. Without the guns/dueling aspect, it’d be just another “build a bigger money engine” game and, I think, would be pretty much indistinguishable.  This game is all about shooting holes in each other’s engines as much as building your own. I can see some people not liking this much confrontational peanut butter, however, in their Caylus chocolate.

I like the way buildings rotate (increasing their income) based on what’s around them.  This makes for a dynamic board with ways to build your income and mess with others’.  Each role has a money cap such that you may take a role and make a lot of money with it, but you can't keep more than the cap allows you. It creates a nice limitation that you need to take into consideration when planning your turn.  There are lots of ways to score VPs, so you can pick a path, but you’ll need to fight for it at some point.  Lots of tension as you try to keep your head down and hope others get into it while leaving you alone.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
At the end of the learning session, me and my friends were already giving Carson City a place among the best games we had ever played. It certainly is the most thematic eurogame I know, with the shootings, the invasion of buildings and the organic growth of the city, which will show a logical and credible map at the end of the game. The core mechanism is a Caylus-inspired action track, but there is a mishmash of others here: role selection, worker placement, tile placement, area control and dice resolution of disputes; but they all fit together in a surprisingly intuitive way. Production quality is excellent, with sturdy components, cowboy meeples and the art of Alexandre Roche. This is probably the best worker placement game I have played so far, on a totally different league from [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/110327/lords-of-waterdeep]other ones[/url] whose much larger popularity baffles me. It is severely underrated in the BGG rank, and deserves to be reprinted.

RATING: 2
8 Word Review:
Fairly symmetrical Euro. Gunfights for worker placement. Meh.

Played 1 time. Rating history: 4
5p - 1

June 2014: Warren taught this to four new players. Teaching took about 20 mins (and W did a good job, I thought), and the gameplay that followed just didn't seem to justify having so much rule complexity. Also, with 5 players there was a horrific amount of downtime between turns, and downtime is something I particularly dislike in games. Maybe I'd enjoy this with 3 players (limiting downtime significantly), but I feel like one play of this was enough for me.

I saw the fun things it has to offer (gunfights for worker placement conflict), and I was fairly meh on the other main mechanics of the game (tile placement, unique roles every round - both have been done better in other games in my opinion). It's just one more parallel play euro to me.

I'd rather play:
Way out West for a western-themed game (differentiated buildings),
Suburbia for a tile laying game (lots of building effects),
Eclipse for a dice-battling game (more customization),

RATING: 9
An excellent worker placement game with some striking similarities to Caylus, but different enough that it still deserves its own place in my collection.  While I slightly prefer Caylus, there is a lot that's different in this game that I like:
- It's about half the length of Caylus
- There's a strong spatial element as Carson City is built up from a small hamlet to a thriving western city as tiles are laid down throughout the game. Deciding where tiles are placed is hugely important.
- There's significant luck in this game, which I actually like, especially combined with the fact that it can be easily mitigated. There are enough spots where dice comes into play that there's a good chance it will balance itself out, making luck less of a factor rather than more.
- The added interaction of the duels is pretty cool. I like an extra helping of direct conflict in my euros.
- The game is saturated in theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

RATING: 4
If you disregard the duels Carson City is a pretty standard, run-of-the-mill worker placement game. The spatial element of building the city on the board is something I haven't seen before but still feels somewhat rough around the edges.

Staking your claims and expanding the city is mainly a question of optimisation and requires careful planning. When other players can ruin the best plan with a lucky roll of the dice, though, that quickly ceases to be fun, especially if you'll sit by the sidelines for the rest of the 90 minutes afterwards. For a game of this length and depth the duels are just completely out of place. Without them, however, the game is nothing special at all.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The old (wild wild) west. I bought this game based only upon the theme and it didn't disappoint me.

Each player choses a different character each turn. Each character has it's own ability.

Carson City, like many other games, functions through actions the players have to chose from. Each action (exceptions are made) can only be played once. If multiple players desire the same action they duel for it. The outcome of the duel desides which players gets to play the action but also infuences the outcome of future duels.

Basically you get to create a town of your own in the old west. Other players get to influence the income you create with the buildings you place on the board.

Each time we play Carson City we have a good time. However I rate this game at a seven rather than an eight because the game is better played with three or four players rather than two. And most of the times we play, it's with two players.....
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

There is no room to hold love in my heart while I'm holdin' a gun in my hand. Die Master Version von Quined mit Alexander Roche in der Design-Kutsche sieht super aus, die Erweiterungen mischen diverse Komponenten neu.

RATING: 8
With the few Wild West games out there, Carson City really fills the niche.  It really keeps with the theme of building out a city with all the old-timey types of buildings you would expect.  All the rules feel integrated and balanced and although it appears as a Caylus ripoff, it feels as much as Caylus as Thunderstone feels to Dominion.  It really is its own game and has a lot of smarts going for it.  There's a bit of AP that goes on due the number of choices to make, but for the type of people that like this openness (like me!) this really isn't too much of a problem (as long as you have patient friends). Can be a mean game with all the duels!

[b]Carson City[/b] [i]Xabier Georges 2009[/i]
Carson City was at first just another eurogame for me, quite similar to Caylus and with nothing special. I did hate the duels using the dice, and I considered it a bad Caylus with some ameritrash touchs. Stupid me. After some time I used the non dice duel variant and I understood the value of the diferent buildings and the risk of duels and then I liked it. Different than Caylus, fast game with a lot of western feeling (way more than Homesteaders, the other Western game I own). Carson City is more difficult to play correctly and I think it is a game more for gamers than non gamers, but its theme helps a lot when you want to try it with a non gamers group.

RATING: 7
Joc de col·locació de treballadors al estil Caylus i de col·locació de losetes en un mapa. Té molta interacció, permetent a més d'un jugador colocar treballadors en un mateix lloc i decidint qui realitzarà l'acció mitjançant un "duel". A més, incorpora elecció de rols al principi de la ronda i ofereix moltes maneres diferents de puntuar, el que augmenta el nombre de posibles estrategies.

Molt "sesudo", pot tendir al "AP" si es vol optimitzar cada acció. El joc bàsic amb daus per resoldre els duels pot resultar massa atzarós, la variant amb losetes és molt millor.

Mathtrade Invierno '13 (a canvi de Rune Age + Expansió)

Actualització després de més partides: el joc empitjora molt amb 5 jugadors, hi ha molt més caos i pots quedar fora de la partida el primer torn si et fan fora de un parell d'accions. A 3-4 jugadors em continua semblant un bon joc.

Té molta interacció, permetent a més d'un jugador colocar treballadors en un mateix lloc i decidint qui realitzarà l'acció mitjançant un "duel". A més, incorpora elecció de rols al principi de la ronda i ofereix moltes maneres diferents de puntuar, el que augmenta el nombre de posibles estrategies.

Molt "sesudo", pot tendir al "AP" si es vol optimitzar cada acció. El joc bàsic amb daus per resoldre els duels pot resultar massa atzarós, la variant amb losetes és molt millor.

Actualització després de més partides: el joc empitjora molt amb 5 jugadors, hi ha molt més caos i pots quedar fora de la partida el primer torn si et fan fora de un parell d'accions. A 3-4 jugadors em continua semblant un bon joc.

RATING: 7

RATING: 6
During my first try at this, I suddenly realized how fast this game is... there are only 4 turns!  There are so many options each turn that it confuses the issue that unlike other worker placement games (Caylus, Agricola, Le Havre), there is no time to do anything but grab points as fast as you can.  You can't develop a long-term strategy, because the game will end before you can set it up.

For example, if you don't get a ranch down on the first turn, it's seriously debatable whether you should ever get one.

I'm not sure I'll ever get to play this game enough to test out all the different strategies, especially since it takes a lot of explaining to introduce newbies to the game.

RATING: 5
This is NOT a brain burner, and could easily be accessible to the entire family.  It may be a little long to explain initially, perhaps due to the impact each Building/House placement has on the value of adjacent Buildings.

This impact makes the game quite interesting, as the denser the town's core, the more expensive it is to buy land at that location!  At the same time, it's a great spot for the placement of a Bank or Saloon!

The forced placement of houses whenever buildings are placed (except for Mines and Ranches), makes this system work quite nicely.

"Role selection" is fairly straight forward, as the character you select will give you a bonus of some sort, but will also limit the amount of cash you may carry over from turn to turn...

One of the finer aspects of this game is that you are never out of the running, there is always a different approach or strategy you can use!  Multiple combos are open to you (Saloon/Hotel, Drugstore/Ranches, Bank/Mines, etc), and you'll also find spaces to generate income should you be short.

The "road" approach (actions are conducted one space at a time along the "road") makes for easy turn resolution, and each space is placed intelligently!  For example, buying land occurs before you may purchase buildings, and income only comes AFTER these buying phases, so you better have kept some money from the previous turn.  But wait, after income you may purchase the all important points!  What to do, what to do?!

And I almost forgot, there are also "Show downs" a.k.a duels, as only ONE player may perform each action (except for 2 which may be performed by multiple players), so if more than one player placed a cowboy at a location, the duel is conducted by a single die roll (adding your "fire power" rating as a bonus).  The winner may conduct the action, the looser(s) receive their cowboy, which may be used at a later turn, and which immediately provides you with a "fire power" bonus.

Ohh, and there are only four turns...

There is one thing I really dislike, and it's the fact that very few points are scored in the few 2-3 rounds, so you end up with a large amount of points scored in the last round... Makes for a rather un-rewarding ending.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Generally, I am not really a big fan of worker placement game. Carson City surprised me - it is actually very fun. It takes a game to become familiar with rules, especially the ones related to the various buildings. It is not really compliated, and I would say it is easier to teach than a lot of other games, such as Puerto Rico.

This game is also great for 2 players, although tension and competition increase significantly with 3-4 players, as you are forced into more duels.

The game has multiple roads to victory, and replay value seems high, given the random location of the city center and the mountains.

####一、初始設置####
1. 每人$15，1條路，12個地塊，3個牛仔，1把左輪
2 .1個牧場放在$3建築位置，另一個放在$10位置
3.1個礦場放在$4建築位置，另一個放在$12位置
4. 從袋中隨機取3個建築放在$5、$6、$8位置
5. 扔骰決定市中心，放1房子，四邊各一條路；扔骰決定9座山的位置
6. 隨機決定順序，每個人放兩塊地，按順序放一個地塊，再按反順序放一個地塊
####二、 放牛仔####
    a 選擇一個行動
    b 選擇一塊地
    c 攻擊、防禦一個建築
    d 放棄，並將自己的順序標記移動到下排最左邊的空位。
####3 執行行動和對決####
  按版圖上循序執行行動。上面的牛仔棄掉。
  如果有多個玩家將牛仔放在一塊地上或行動上，對決開始。
  對決＝玩家的牛仔數＋左輪數＋骰子數。大者勝，贏家的牛仔棄掉，輸家將牛仔放回自己保留處。平手時看玩家順序。
(變體規則:用決鬥版塊替換骰子。玩這個變體規則需要使用如下改動。
!!遊戲設置時: 2人遊戲, 每人得到從0到5共6個決鬥版塊和 $15。
!!3人遊戲到5人遊戲, 每個玩家拿取從0到5共6個決鬥板塊，洗混。每人隨機秘密拿取6個決鬥版塊。
每個玩家公佈自己決鬥版塊的總點數。總數必須是45 (3人遊戲), 60 (4人遊戲) 或 75 (5人遊戲)。 每個玩家得到（30-自己的決鬥總點數）的錢作為起始現金。
!!!!!!如何進行決鬥:每個玩家選擇並打出一個決鬥版塊以增加自己的活力點來取代扔骰子。決鬥版塊使用後丟棄作為新的供應區。當一個玩家的決鬥版塊用盡時，從供應區隨機拿娶3個新的決鬥版塊。!!!!!!!
4回合結束
 回合標記向左一格。 每個玩家得到牛仔，第1回合結束時，每人4個，第2、第3回合，每人5個。牛仔上限10個。
超過角色現金上限的錢必須花掉。$10=1分。
沒有被購買的建築向左移動。摸袋子補全建築。
 
行動表（按順序）
注意：玩家也可以選擇不做這個行動,即使對決獲勝。
(a) $4，不可對決
(b) 彈藥，得到3把左輪，回合結束時歸還
(c )3條路
(d)1條路，不可對決
(e) 購買無主之地，
###地價＝1＋自己和周圍3×3格中的建築、山、房子數###
(f) 建造建築 
    f.1 除了牧場和礦場，建築的一角要通到路。玩家可以造任意多路。
    f.2 放藥店、礦場、銀行、沙龍、教堂、監獄時，玩家要放一個房子在沒有建築/山的、有路連接的地塊，可以是無主或自己的或別人的（需要對方同意)
   ### 注意：玩家可以保留該建築以後再造，到一下次建造建築動作時再造。
(g) 地塊收入，玩家的每塊地$2
(h)## 牛仔收入，玩家的牛仔＋左輪，每個$2
(i) 賭博收入，玩家扔2個骰子，得到骰子之和的收入
(j) 建築收入，玩家的每個建築得到收入，成功攻擊玩家建築的牛仔偷到該建築的一半收入（向下取整），並棄掉。
    注意：屬於某玩家的房子只會惠及該玩家，無主地上的房子惠及所有玩家。山脈對礦場的效果同理。
(h) 地塊得分，每2個地塊1分
(i) 牛仔得分，牛仔＋左輪，每2個一分
(j) 建築得分，玩家地塊上的每個建築1分（房子和山脈除外）
(k) 購買分數，可以分別以$5,$4,$3,$2的價格換取分數
 
遊戲結束時：
每個建築、房子、山脈得2分
玩家手中剩下的錢以$6的價格換取分數
分數最高的玩家獲勝，平手時看玩家順序。
 
建築
房子：需要有路連接
牧場：收入：每個空地$1，＋1左輪，計算藥店、銀行、沙龍的收入時看作1個房子。
藥店：需要有路連接，玩家要放置一個房子。收入：每個臨近的房子或自己任意位置的牧場$3
礦場：收入：每個臨近的山脈$3，＋1左輪
銀行：需要有路連接，玩家要放置一個房子。收入：每個臨近的房子或自己任意位置的礦場$3
沙龍：需要有路連接，玩家要放置一個房子。收入：每個臨近的房子$5
旅館：需要有路連接，玩家要放置一個房子。收入：$6,計算藥店、銀行、沙龍的收入時看作2個房子。
教堂：需要有路連接，玩家要放置一個房子。收入：無。計算藥店、銀行、沙龍的收入時看作1個房子。教堂和周圍的建築不可被攻擊。
監獄：需要有路連接，玩家要放置一個房子。收入：無。+2左輪，不可被攻擊。
 
角色
1 警長：獲得白色meeple，它不會被攻擊。但是也不能用它攻擊別人。變體:不能挑起決鬥只能防禦，每場輸了拿3分。現金上限$20
2 銀行家：+9$ ，變體錢換分。現金上限$120
3 雜貨商：+8$或選擇一種建築，其收入加倍。現金上限$60
4 中國苦力：獲得2條路，所有建築造價減半（向上取整）， 變體:每棟房子一分。現金上限$30
5 定居者：立刻獲得一塊地，必須是無主的。變體:8元或1山1元現金上限$30
6 船長：付1$得到一個牛仔或4$得到兩個牛仔或付9$得到三個牛仔。變體:花錢買槍，現金上限$25
7 雇傭兵：多3把左輪。變體 暫時加2槍。現金上限$20

«強權既是公理» 變體 
這個變體提議減少決鬥中的隨機性， 用決鬥版塊替換骰子。玩這個變體規則需要使用如下改動。
遊戲設置時: 
2人遊戲, 每人得到從0到5共6個決鬥版塊和 $15。

3人遊戲到5人遊戲, 每個玩家拿取從0到5共6個決鬥板塊，洗混。每人隨機秘密拿取6個決鬥版塊。
每個玩家公佈自己決鬥版塊的總點數。總數必須是45 (3人遊戲), 60 (4人遊戲) 或 75 (5人遊戲)。 每個玩家得到（30-自己的決鬥總點數）的錢作為起始現金。

進行決鬥:每個玩家選擇並打出一個決鬥版塊以增加自己的活力點來取代扔骰子。決鬥版塊使用後丟棄作為新的供應區。當一個玩家的決鬥版塊用盡時，從供應區隨機拿3個新的決鬥版塊。

«卡森河»變體 遊戲設置時 :
• 所有河流區域必須空置(不能有山地和城市中心); 
• 6 座山脈版塊取代原有的9座。
###既不允許購買河流區域也不允許在河流區域上建造建築或房屋。
計算農場收入時河流區域被視為空閒區域(因此,每個河流區域 +$1)。
###如果一個礦井與河流相鄰，礦井增加 $3收入，無論有多少 
###河流區域與其相鄰。穿越河流必須要有2條路。穿越河流的2條路被稱為橋。
遊戲結束時, 任何與橋相鄰的區域 (包含山、建築或房屋)  i帶來 3 分取代原有的2分。

RATING: 6
What a shame this wasn't invented before Caylus. The picking actions along a track mechanism has been lifted wholesale, but the building takes place on a 2D grid.  Thematically it's richer than its predecessor, but the die rolling for 'gunfights' does add extra randomness. The scoring is tight after four rounds, so winning really amounts to not wasting any actions in fights you can't win. The sheriff role is ridiculously powerful given that it guarantees you first action and an undefeatable placement to put on the best cash-vp conversion space. In all, lighter and more fun than Caylus, but less determinism in the outcome.

RATING: 7.3
Un juego con mucho potencial que parece inacabado. Lo situaría entre Stone Age y Agricola en una escala de dificultad. Combina varios mecanismos (elección de roles, colocación de trabajadores...) pero al contrario que otros "juegos-compendio" el conjunto no es superior a las partes. El núcleo del juego son los duelos (dos o más trabajadores situados en una misma casilla de acción se baten para ver quién la realiza) que se resuelven con unas losetas repartidas al azar numeradas de 0 a 5 (junto con otros modificadores). Esto obliga a pensar en varias estrategias, lo que sin duda no es malo. Puntos fuertes: hay interacción "de la de verdad", elevada rejugabilidad (dos tableros, dos variaciones de cada personaje, disposición inicial aleatoria...), perdona más que Caylus y Agricola. Contras: explicación compleja, algo corto para mi gusto.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First played on January 26th, 2011. Taught in 10min, played in 2 hours with 4 players. Each player begins with some money, a gun, a road, 3 workers and a plot to be placed. The grid is setup with default town center and mountains. Game is played over 4 rounds with scoring at the end of each round. At the start of each round, players get additional pawns from their supply too. Players choose a role that determines play order later and peforms a special ability as well as $ limits at the end of each turn. (1-Sherrif; special pawn can't be placed with, 2-Banker; $9, 3-Factory Worker; $8 or 2x money in scoring, 4-Builder; 50% off buildings and 2 free roads, 5-Farmer; one free plot, 6-General; one/$1, two/$4, or three/$9 additional workers from supply, 7-Outlaw; 3 guns). Next players place their pawns one at a time on the item track. If they pass they go in line to determine order for grabbing roles next turn. Each player may have different amounts of pawns. Once all players place their pawns (or pass) then determine who wins items. Some items are not fought over and are awarded ($4 or 1 road). Others are battled over if multiple colored pawns exist (3 guns, 3 roads, etc). Players each roll a dice and add that number to the amount of guns they have to determine who wins and who send their meeple back to their play area (not the general supply). Other spaces are for tiles (mines, bank, ranch, salon, prison) to be placed on the board, $ (based on plots owned, guns, or dice roll) or VP (so many per plot, guns, buildings and $). Players may also placed pawns on the grid to make a claim on a location (may also be fought over). Players may make claim to an unclaimed house or mountain worth 2VPs at the game end. To place a tile, a road must be placed such that the road exends continous from the city and touch at least a corner of the tile. The tile must also be placed on an already claimed lot. Tiles are worth various points per mountain, mine, empty lot, house, etc. Some tiles have a red house designating that it should be scored as a house. Some tiles allow you to place a house when the tile is placed. Tiles are rotated such that the VPs are represented based on the number of houses, etc. At the end, the player the the most VPs wins. I'd recommend this game for those who like more complex games with lots of options. It tends to be a slower developing game with lots of decisions to be made. For those wanting more fast pace gameplay, may want to look elsewhere.

RATING: 3.5
Carson City was massive disappointment for me because I am a fan of the designer's other games, particularly Ginkgopolis and Troyes. A primary distinctive game design element in Carson City is the ability to challenge other players' workers they have already placed by placing one of your own on the same space, which will create a duel. Each player counts the guns they currently have plus the number of unused workers and adds 1d6. Highest total gets to do the action, loser gets their worker back with no work done. Ties fall to turn order. I hate this new take on worker placement, where players are not excluding one another from actions, but have to be prepared to get jacked but subsequent players every time you place a worker. You better be strapped. This is a mean game. Also, the die rolling adds a great degree of randomness as well. I did not enjoy Carson City at all. I will try it again one day.

RATING: 8.5
Buenísimo euro con montón de interacción.

Se trata de un juego de colocación de trabajadores ambientado en Oeste. Desde los materiales hasta los duelos hacen que el tema no se sienta pegado. Cada jugador tiene unos vaqueros que va colocando en las acciones que se pueden realizar, pudiendo ir más de un jugador a realizar esa acción, por lo que se producirá un duelo. 

Para mi, muy divertido. Eso si jugando con los dados para resolver el duelo, esa pizca de tensioncilla es muy de agradecer.

Además al inicio de cada turno se elige un personaje que te da una habilidad y un límite de dinero a guardar de turno a turno. Y es que esa es otra cosa que está muy bien hecha. Se gana cambiando pasta por puntos, siempre se tiene un cambio de 10 a uno pero hay cada ronda unos espacios de acción que quien se lo lleva puede hacer un cambio mucho mejor, a pelearse por ellos tocan ...

Tan solo por los personajes que incluye la expansión Gold & Guns ya merece la pena. Abre un mundo de estrategias por la cantidad de sinergias que tienen los personajes con las acciones

La expansión de customización de inicio es una vuelta de tuerca más. Solo para jugadores que conozcan bien el juego porque una mala elección al inicio te puede pasar mucha factura

El personaje del Indio me mola, me parece curioso

RATING: 7.5
Worker placement:role selection:city building:screwage:blind bid(combat non-dice rule)

DO NOT PLAY WITH THE DICE PEOPLE HELLO?  

Have you not learnt anything from the old RISK days? Monopoly? Do you really love dice and luck?

Really two ways to play and you choose the HIGH luck version and complain after?  NINCOMPOOPS! :P


Yes well, now that I got that out of my system there is a really nice little system here. Don't believe the haters, mostly unwarranted complaints is all I see so far although people who dont like some screwage might just do better to avoid this (its not really high but its there to be sure)

Yes there is a little Caylus with how the road resolves in order and of course the worker placement but the game does not feel like Caylus at all and the theme really comes out very nice in this game, I was put off by the Caylus comparisons but to me this is a non-issue and the game feels quite original even though there is nothing really new. 

So in the old west players are fighting for land and good land plots and trying to gain as much income as possible over only 4 rounds with only 4 - 8 cowboys to use per round. In turn order roles are selected to help you in the round and then buildings are laid out on the road. Players can attempt to buy the buildings (sliding scale) but other players can also try to claim the same building. This creates a duel and a player adds up his fire power and plays one of his handful of tiles (numbered 0 to 5, randomly distributed, slight bluffing aspect, extra money given for low gun count) against another to see who will buy the building, loser gets his worker back to use in the next round and it counts in the current round as +1 fire power. Note that these duels can be a little tricky to judge in the first round (there shouldnt be many anyway) but as the game progresses they usually become easier to calculate and can even be quite deterministic. Losing can hurt for sure even though you get compensated and that is part of the theme and game, it stung me more in my second game than the first and it hurt my income. If you are low on guns planning can be pretty tricky since you are never sure which things might be fought after, this might mean passing first to get first pick of character in next round to help with this.  

Cowboys can also claim new land plots and these are needed to build the buildings on. Certian spots are more lucrative than others due to surrounding buildings but only neutral and your own buildings will help. Buildings generate income and at the end of each round there is an option to buy VP's if you visited the "VP store"(hehe) and did not lose a duel there.  Buildings are also subject to duels so if someone is making a pile of money you can try to get your share unless 1 of the 2 churches is built near by which ensures peace. One thing I noticed is by taking the Sheriff someone can lock down the only VP spot left in the last round, because only 2 characters let you keep more than 30 dollars, this seems like it "could" be too strong but possibly not. You can buy up land for points but this costs you one action per land, more play is needed to see if this is balanced. Again careful planning is needed here as you dont want to be throwing away money or doing lots of high conversion to VP's.  

Essentially thats the game.  Its a nice mix of strategy, tactics, theme, high interaction and fun all in 90 Minutes. It looks like there might be multiple paths here, Guns look very important but someone won our last game with only ONE gun LOSING most duels so planning is rewarded here and there are other ways to get VP's like how much land you own, etc. The role that doubles your land seems very powerful and you might have to "take one for the team" to stop someone from getting too much income, wasnt crazy about this one aspect (not sure how big it is yet) but there are a couple other ways to try and combat it as well like stealing the income. 4 most likely the best number for this, 3 and 5 should be okay but 4 best I think (just the right amount of competition)

RATING: 7
First of all, I want to say that I only played with the yellow sides of the characters, but if they were the only ones that existed then I'd probably give the game a 6. So the 7 rating is speculative and based on using the red sides of the characters.

The game itself is a fairly simple worker placement, get money through board actions and through building buildings that generate income, and then buy points with that money, at a rate that gets worse as the game goes on. Actually you can only convert money to points if you take one of those worker placement spots, which slowly leave the board as the game goes on. And otherwise you have a max amount of money you can take into the next round, and any excess gets traded to points at an awful rate. There's some fun to be had on the board figuring out how and where to build the buildings to get income. But the problem is there's really only the one way to get points, to buy them. There are a few board spots that might be worth a couple of points by the end of the game, but that's a pretty small portion of the points you'll be getting. The twist on worker placement here is that you can kick people out of spots that they get to first if you duel them. But the problem with the yellow sides of the characters is that the person who gets the sheriff gets first choice of a spot that can't be dueled, and then the outlaw comes in and puts himself in a position where you basically can't beat him in a duel, so he gets whatever else he wants. And then the other roles are a bit underwhelming. On the red sides the outlaw gets less firepower and the sheriff instead of getting an extra, unduelable worker gets an extra point scoring opportunity. And most of the also-rans from the yellow sides also get unique point scoring opportunities on the red side, which seems like it would make the game a bit more varied than just maximizing income and then taking either sheriff or outlaw to get one of the good conversion spaces.

RATING: 6.5
Rating might be unfair.  This rubbed me the wrong way because of the semi-convoluted payment system.  There's a helpful player aid, but it's missing a crucial set of information - which buildings score only when they're adjacent to you (vs. anywhere on the board), and which buildings score if they're not owned by either player.  That made for a LOT of stopping to consult the rules, a LOT more AP than necessary, etc.

Also, I'm pretty sure we were not doing something right, because we didn't have a single duel in our 2P game.  Too many other things to get done in the limited amount of time.

Pros: game length (aside from AP issues) was generally good.  Theme was well integrated - the city expanded as it should have.  TONS of replay value here, even absent all the chrome rules and variants (due to the board setup, and the way building tiles come out.)

Cons: aside from what I noted above, the components are a bit dodgy (board is bubbling in a couple spots where it wasn't glued perfectly).  Typical Eagle Games fare.  

It's quite possible I play this a couple times more, everything clicks, and it becomes a 7.5 or an 8.  But it also might not hit the table again after that first experience.

RATING: 7
2014-06-17

Initial Rating: 7.5 (September 2010)

It’s been years since I played Carson City and I want to play it again soon to see if I can appreciate it better.

I remember I liked the game, very reminiscent of Caylus (a game I love) BUT at the end Carson City left a bittersweet taste because I didn’t really like the luck inherent in the duels. In a WP game when you take an action you should secure that action IMO but that’s not the case in Carson City (I know many people love this feature). Here, you can fight for actions and you can use guns to better your odds but still I have seen a player with many guns lose because he rolled a one and the opponent rolled a six. To be clear I’m not against  fighting over spaces (love Vanuatu), I’m against the luck of the duel die that (somewhat) prevents you to plan ahead with some confidence. I have to play again to see if this continue to be an issue for me or not.

The theme is nice, but it doesn’t do anything for me as I have zero interest in it. The game components are outstanding and I love the role selection. 

To conclude, Carson City is a good WP game. I need to play again soon to properly comment as I have forgotten many features of the game. For now, I’m rating it according to my current feelings toward the game, but Carson City has a lot of potential so it will probably go up in my personal ratings. I have been trying to buy it, but nowadays it’s hard to get.

2016-01-25

At last I got to play Carson City again and I enjoyed it. The duels continue to bother me, not only because there are some luck inherent in them, but because all players can gang on a player, usually the perceived leader and it is very hard to do well when all other players are dueling you for your spaces all the time. I have fallen out of love of this mechanism (take that mechanism) because I´m the target 99% of the time, even if I´m in last position :angry:. Still I enjoy the game and the luck factor can be (somewhat) controlled with the firepower (guns and unplaced cowboys). Also when you lose a duel it isn’t the end of the world, you will get your cowboy back for the next round, so to summarize, I feel losing a duel isn’t as damaging as I thought before.

I love the variable setup adding to the replayability factor and I also love that the characters have another facet. I want to play with the red side soon.

Bottom line, I enjoy Carson City and, due to this, I’m upgrading its rating. It is however a borderline 7.0 for me because the duels still bother me a bit but I recently found out there is an official variant to decrease even further the luck of the duels. I want to try it.

One of 2009´s favorites.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
There are two key differences between this and other worker placement games. The first is that most actions can be contested. If two or more players select the same action, they have a gunfight to determine who gets to take it. The losers get their cowboys back to play on the following turn and the winner gets the action. Your firepower is the modifier on your roll, and firepower can be increased by owning certain buildings, selecting a gun-totin' personality, or winning the "ammunition" action.

The other key difference is the town-building board play. Buildings confer certain benefits, mostly providing income but sometimes increased firepower or defense. You also score points for buildings and money at game's end. Buildings earn money based on their proximity to other types of buildings and so there are some interesting choices in planning and placement.

The game actually reminded me of Stone Age quite a bit, another medium-weight worker placement game with dice. I prefer Carson City, though, because there is a greater range of options and each decision feels more significant (also, I despise the massive and largely hidden end-game scoring of Stone Age). I think they basically have the same audience. Carson City would be a reasonable step up for someone who enjoys Stone Age and is looking for something similar, but a bit more strategic. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Strategies to be followed are restricted and randomness in duels is high, but they are funny. This and a short time of play make the game enjoyable.

RATING: 7.2
The mechanics and theme match very well - I really like how it all works.  It's fun seeing the town expand - that's very neat - the houses arrive automatically as buildings are built.  The closest game to it is probably Age Of Empires III, which is another favourite of mine. Caylus is another obvious comparison game but it just 'felt' closer to AOE3, perhaps because of the more conscious integration of theme and mechanics. I think that this also makes the game just a little clunky in places - calculating income is a bit annoying, for example - but I'm willing to forgive that. Importantly, the game IS fast. It does not wear out its welcome and some might argue that it finishes too quickly - I will have to reserve judgement for now.  The rules are easy to understand but I think that they could have used a bit more flavour, which I added when explaining. For example, the action where you get $4 per cowboy on the space is 'obviously' where the cowboys simply go working as labourers for the turn to generate a bit of extra cash.

RATING: 8.2
I rank this and World Without End as my two favorite worker placement games.

I liked:
- The ability to duel for placement spots, as opposed to just blocking other players out as in most other placement games.
- The "arc" of development and growth of Carson City as we build
- The feeling that I've only scratched the surface of various strategies; there's so much to do and so many ways to get victory points
- The theme that pervades everything - personality abilities, guns & duels, building requirements and earnings... (eating chili & cornbread while we played added to the immersive experience nicely)
- The biggest, chunkiest, best pair of wooden dice I've ever seen.

I get the same feeling as I do in Hansa Teutonica that to do well you need to be very flexible and respond to what other players are doing. You need to adjust your tactics and strategy to what you see on the board, and what will work in one game might not in another.

RATING: 5
As in his other game Palais Royal, Xavier Georges created a worker placement game with a twist and couples it with a spatial part. The spatial part of placing buildings to construct a western city is unpolished and fell flat for me as did the palace garden in Palais Royal. Xavier Georges goes on my list of not-to-watch authors.
