RATING: 9
A worker placement game with lots of player interaction and more theme than other euros. It's sometimes called Caylus with a theme, and while not entirely fair, it does resonate.

What distinguishes Carson City from most worker placement games is not theme, but that all actions are always available to all players. However, the cost of these actions may change depending what players decide to do. If you really want a particular action, or parcel of land, and another player has put their worker on it, you can put your worker there too. A gunfight will ensue and the winner claims the spot. 

Worker placement is essentially a timing driven bidding mechanic. You bid by placing your workers, which denies other players that spot. In effect, bidding earlier is how you bid higher. 

Carson City changes the importance of timing. If you want a particular action, or parcel of land, you don't need to place your worker their first. You can place after and challenge them to a duel. Placing first signals your intent, though it may act as a deterrent. So if you wait to place your cowboy on a spot you can still place it there, you just might have to pay more for, where "pay" means committing more firepower, and the risk of losing. So the cost for each place on the board is variable rather than fixed. 

This is reminiscent of the displacement action in Hansa Teutonica, or placing knights and squires in Lancaster. The way it works here is different, but the idea is the same: you can do whatever you want so long as you're willing to pay for it.

I can see playing this 50+ times.

UPDATE: I have about 10 plays in cardboard and another 20 plays on Yucata. This still holds up well, however I have come to prefer it with the river, the might-is-right variant, and using random combinations of the personalities. My preference would be to have all that and randomly choose personalities from the base game and the expansion. That would give take this to 100+ plays. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
I personally really like this game.  I love a lot about the game especially the theme behind the game itself.  Everything is very fitting, and the type of game (worker placement) is one of my favorite.  Other enjoyable factors include the way player turns are determined, game based mostly on skill over luck, and replay value due to random starting positions.

I would personally rate this game a lot higher, maybe in the 8 - 9 range if I had people as equally thrilled to play this game as myself.  I've only been lucky enough to play a few full fledged 4P games and not enough to really develop a deep connection with it.

So the lack of full points on this game comes purely on the fact that it's not drawing my friends together to play as much as some of the other games in my collection.  That is a huge part of what a game needs to be no matter how much I want to play it.  Since this isn't a solo game, I must detract points for that alone.

The other factor is the fact that the one aspect I wish was more used in the game isn't really used at all if any.  That's the cowboy taking over someone else's spot.  As with all my other games, we love cut throat games and stick it to the other person as much as possible.  The pure joy of being the reason why someone loses feels good, and I personally expected that the gun slinging would lead to a few game swings.  However, it's more or less not even visible in a normal run of the game.  Since the risk factor and payout does not balance well, most players play the game like Sim City and concentrate on just their own doings since they are in full control.  This sadden me a bit since there wasn't much sticking it to other players as I'd like.

Overall, I suggest this game for anyone who is down for worker placement games like myself.  I would give it way more plays if the group of friends I have wouldn't mind the time investment over the other choices of games I own.

RATING: 7.5

solid euro with interesting options and smooth gameplay.

session report by me
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/4137935

Update after playing gunslinger and way out west.

Downgraded it from 8 to 7.5
actually I have a feeling that this might be the weakest of the three. 
Gunslinger I liked but there are not so many options really compared to the others. It's all about shooting
Way out west Should be played with at least four players. 
Early negative rolls can have a devestating effect on your income and put someone in a strong position

Carson city played well with three. It has the least connection to the theme than the other two. But there was some room to get back in the game.

Now scoring on execution of gunfight

Gunslinger 9
Way out west 7
Carson city 4.

Way out west and carson city have an economic aspect to it as well. While in Way out west there is no option but to fight yourself back in the game. There are some actions (or characters that will provide you with the income.
While looking at the theme there is no need to recover.

In gunbslinger I got a bad aim since I was shot in my gun hand  so I lost (there were some cards that could have allowed me to win) but it would have been  a lucky shot.

In way out west it is possible that when  you get beaten in a fight even though you are the favourite you will loose a part of your income. It's very hard to make up for it if it happens early on.  But I'm okay with that it feels real.

In carson city character selection and action selection will provide you with income to stay competetive . Losing a fight doesn't have such a huge impact for that reason. But to me that is a flaw in a game depicting the wild west.
Nonetheless I think of carson city as my settlers. This game I can easily explain to non-gamers and  have a good time playing it.

RATING: 6
Initial one-game-played -rating. Wow, this has a lot of different mechanics crammed together. Maybe too much, this needs more plays to find out. The tile-laying spatial aspect combined with worker placement works great, not too sure about the role selection though. It seems that the dominant strategies are either firepower supremacy or avoiding gunfights +using the trader to double income (to my surprise I won the game with the latter along with going first for cash to points -conversion).

Update: Some of the roles are obvious choices while others are useless most of the time, so not much game there. Still, despite its shortcomings (mainly that one or two key dice rolls can in a worst case make or break your game, which feels out of place in worker placement, pun intended) it's somewhat interesting, and doesn't take too long even with more players. Being hosed by dice in a 2 hour game would be too much.

Want to try the New Beginning variant.

RATING: 4
It's a worker placement game, and like all worker placement game, either the make little sense with the them, either they scale badly with the number of players.

What is unique in carson city is that competition for the same space is resolve in a duel which consist in a die roll + the number of guns you have. That is an interesting mechanic and method to add randomness to a worker placement game.

It's a fair mechanic untill somebody has 7 guns making it easier to get the level 3 gun and ending up with 10 guns + characters and killing everything in sight.

Else the game is averagly complex and not color blind friendly because each swuare not only contains a building but also the color of the player who owns the terrain. So the board eventually get pretty messed up.

RATING: 9
Very good game. Sleeper hit from 2009-2010. Gameplay is surprisingly deep even though luck plays a big role in the game. But luk can be mitigated if you focus on acquiring "guns". 4 turns is really quick to play and you need to be real careful not to loose the "pace" of the game. One turn where an opponent buys a lot of point can really end the game.

There is also PLENTY of replayability in the game: 2 boards, 3 variants (and a simili-luckless combat variant), role cars with 2 sides... It is really amazing how much is packed in this game.

Also: the theme is incredibly well rendered. The best cowboy game I played since Doomtown CCG.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Caylus? No. Worker Placement and the actions executed in order "like Caylus", yes.

There's the main area where you build, gain multipliers for your income and or points, as well as potentially screw your opponents income.

Then there's the matter of the characters that determine turn order for the round with variable powers; tack onto that that they are chosen in ascending passing order from the previous round.

It's all tense, diverse, and quite fun.

I will say however that the dice can be a little unforgiving, though this can be mitigated and perhaps avoided altogether with the variant. I also do not think this game is as good with 5.

RATING: 6
I really like the theme.  I really like the worker placement track at the top of the board.  And I really like the interaction between players.

I just have a hard time really liking the bottom two thirds of the board and the fiddly tiles you have to lay down.  The mechanics are sometimes confusing and hard to remember.  It gets a little frustrating when you're trying to make a move and the thing you've been working for the last ten minutes isn't allowed because your too close to a mountain or someone else's claim.  Fiddly.

That being said, I enjoy the game enough to play it when it comes to the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is one of the most brain burning games I own, and a pretty unique game to boot. It takes a long time to teach, but it's not actually complicated -- it's just so heavy on options and paths to victory that every turn requires a lot of thought.

There's a fair amount of downtime as you wait for people to make decisions, but it doesn't feel like downtime because you need every second to formulate your own plans. There wasn't much chit-chat during our first play for this same reason -- playing Carson City is an intense, head-down experience.

I found myself exclaiming aloud that it was a fascinating game during our first play. It expresses its themes well (I actually felt like we were founding a city in the Wild West, rough-and-tumble jockeying for position and all), and as a conflict-heavy Euro it occupies a unique spot in my collection.

It could rise to an 8 or 9 after a couple more plays; it's a 7 because one play just isn't enough to really give a full assessment. Unfortunately, one player said he didn't want to play it again (for many of the reasons I enjoyed it), and I suspect another regular player in our group would hate it...so it's not likely to see much action. Bummer!

---

Neat...but three years later its first play was its only play. There are other games I'd rather play. Sold it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It's hard to say how I feel about this game after 1 play. It's heavy. The concept and flow of play are simple enough--perhaps even light. However, the details about building and scoring make this game complex. The instructions are well laid out, and the game board makes good use of iconography, but it still felt a little cloudy during the first play. I think it's similar to Caylus in many ways. The building aspect is similar, the resolution of actions is similar and the weight is on par. I definitely like it. It's more thematic, less dry and more random than Caylus, but it's also less pure. I think it has the makings of a great game, and can see it having legs, for sure.

RATING: 5
Update: tried again, still the same, downgraded. I don't need to play this again, your valuable gaming time can be spent so much better.

Some fun this game, but nothing new here. It's worker placement on a set path like Caylus. The duels are very important and include a considerable random element. I prefer Caylus, which is more strategic, no dice, but I am no fan of this type of worker placement games in general.

RATING: 6
This rating is somewhat tentative.  I've had my eye on Carson City for a while but only recently picked it up.  I've now played twice, and have a couple of concerns.  

Firstly, it seems difficult to avoid one player focusing on guns to the point where on his turn he can do whatever he wants.  This seems to greatly limit the degree to which other players strategies can be executed and more importantly puts the gun-focused player in the position of kingmaker/spoiler if he won't get quite enough points to win.

Secondly, I don't really like that there is a trade-off between building the city and scoring victory points.  The game feels as though you are trying to build the 'best city,' but in reality that seems to mean you've waited too long to start scoring points.

I would still like to play it again, as I certainly don't feel I've explored the game's possibilities to any great extent.

RATING: 8
Sorta a cross between Way Out West and Caylus.  It's a worker placement game where after the worker placement phase actions are resolved in order determined by their position on the board.  What makes this game interesting is that players are also buying and placing tiles to build towns.  There are some interesting placement and timing decisions to consider that can effect your opponents.  Furthermore there are shootouts that determine who wins a tile (if multiple workers are placed on an action) and you can try to steal profits from buildings.  I think I would always prefer the dice for shootouts over the less exciting card-draw variant.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Yet another worker placement euro that presents players with solveable scenarios and demands engine-building/snowball planning like most games of this ilk. Why anyone finds this fun is truly beyond me - it's Excel spreadsheety in the worst way, and to mitigate this it pretends to add a "novel" mechanic of dueling, which offers players a chance to "compete" and roll off for control of a space. But in the end, even in spite of the dice roll, the stronger player (the one who has collected more guns) typically wins. So the game rewards the same snowball planning while simultaneously it punishes risk-taking. This robs the game of any meaningful conflict or tension and what we are left with is an abstract, pasted-on Western theme version of Caylus-light that must make the fanboys in their fatboy underpants squee with delight. No thank you.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Despite playing very differently, Carson City reminded me of Power $truggle. Both adopt an established, popular Euro game mechanism as their core (worker placement and area majority, respectively) and then layer subsystem upon subsystem on top in an effort to build a highly thematic experience: duels, town expansion, and roles in Carson City versus bribes, secret goals, and divisional powers in Power $truggle. The result in both cases is a game that takes forever to explain, seems far more complicated than it really is, but is ultimately fairly straightforward to play. Both are very "thematic" for Euro games, but fans of "Ameritrash" would probably find them quite dry. While I've enjoyed my first play of each, I'm not entirely sure how much of an actual game exists beneath all the moving parts. Definitely interested in trying again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
In this Western worker placement game, your workers can be challenged to a duel for a right to use the action. Duels consist of a dice roll, modified by guns you have and extra workers. 

Each turn, you choose a role that will give you some benefits for the turn, such as extra income or extra firepower. The game lasts 4 turns, where you construct buildings on a map to earn income or VPs. 

This game has a lot of theme. But, you could be picked on, and it is not a nice feeling to lose a bunch of duels or be picked on by the big bully on campus. This game came out in 2009, and I missed it with my gaming group, but I had a chance to play it on yucata.de. It's fun to play for theme, not so much in terms of a strategic game, but perhaps I haven't played enough to be able to defend against attacks. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.9
[b]Carson City[/b]

2-5 players, worker-placement game

The wild west theme suits this game very well. A Caylus worker-placement type of game, but with several twists: 

a) workers can fight over the worker placement spots
b) you are building a city on a square grid map
c) every turn, you choose a role (sheriff, banker, grocer, etc.), which gives certain benefits

Designer Diary:
https://boardgamegeek.com/blogpost/45801/designer-diary-carson-city-or-town-wasnt-built-day

RATING: 3
NOT a strategy game. There are some elements of strategy but they are carefully destroyed by a roll of dice. Indeed, ultimately your decisions will be challenged by a duel with another player and the outcome of the duel will be decided by a dice. True, by carefully planning your moves, you might get some guns which help in a fight. But those moves are likely to be challenged by a duel, and again a roll of dice. The importance of the dice can probably be played down by using a four sided dice, or a longer game (oh god no!) or simply by limiting the game to three players. As it is, at five players every single one of your actions will be challenged by a duel and a dice roll. in a four round game, you get one round of bad luck and your game is fucked. A good thing is that a lost duel is not a lost action, because your cowboy (understand workman) will be given back to you, and you can play it again on the next round. If you lose a lot of duels, you have more actions to play the next round. But that means a duel is a very common place, and ultimately the game turns around provocating duels and therefore rolling dice... and strategy makes place to luck.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The features that separate Carson City from other Worker Placement games happen to be mechanics that are among my least favorite; dice rolling for success/failure outcome, and area control. The impact these mechanics have on gameplay creates a feeling of impermanence to one's actions and purchases. Nothing is safe! Place a worker and he can be removed by a duel. Build a building and its value can be eroded by other player's purchases. This lawlessness might be thematic, but is probably also part of my general dislike of the Old West.

CC is a victory point salad, except you have to commit workers to trigger VP payout, so on top of the concerns listed above, one must also judiciously use their finite workers in this way or much one's work has little value at game end.

Aside from a personal dislike of the game's era, I think CC could be forbidding for new players as some kind of comprehensible strategy may take several games to manifest, which is a dealbreaker for me.

RATING: 8
A nice twist on a worker-placement game. If you go in expecting Caylus-lite you will be sorely disappointed; managing dueling is a key part of this game. At the same time the frisson of randomness reduces the need to make a foolproof plan, which helps reduce AP.

There are some compensations for losing a duel, which makes it not too much of a blow. I enjoy a little bit of chaos too.

At 4 rounds it plays very quickly. I also enjoy the spatial aspect of the town.

The "Kit Carson" variant sounds awesome.

RATING: 7.2
Rules are not complex and the game is dynamic. But the 4 rounds take longer than you initially expect (there are a lot of things to consider and to do!).

Worker placement at heart, but adds role selection, tile laying and interaction (demanded by the theme). The interaction is very important, duels can have deep effects, for good or for bad. They can be played with tiles or dice. I prefer tiles, they add a memory mechanism to the game but are much more strategic and tactic.

The theme implementation is fantastic. It feels a bit like the wild west (and that's a lot to say about an euro, lol).

Art is fine (maybe a bit uninspired) and component quality is good (cowboy meeples! yay! Heavy cardstock!)

It's has a bit more depth than it initially seems, but still it is a game that will please many players.

RATING: 8.4
[2010/01/28]

wow... so i went to a gammenight.. and Brian said "you wanna help me learn carson city?" and i said "sure"
a random guy joined us (we got lucky because it could have been lame with only 2)

so after about an hour of starting to play, Brian was about to give up... the rules are tough to learn... (they're not bad once you know them)
we stuck with it, and made some mistakes along the way, but by the end i was sold...

Perhaps this is not a true strategy game, since there are a lot of dice rolls, but the dice rolls are sooo different than your typical dice rolls... these roll's decide winners/losers of duels... but they DON'T decide winners and losers of the game...

it is done SOOO welll, when you win a duel, you use that dude to buy something... when your lose a duel, that dude returns to your possey to help to kick ass in other duels still to be fought... and he THEN can STILL be used to claim something the next turn... (plus the later duels are usually the ones you want to win anyway)

so you only use dudes to buy stuff you want.. and if you lose... you have a better chance the rest of that turn, and you have an extra move next turn...

it's almost like the majority of duels in this game don't really affect the winner of the game, they just change the way each involed player needs to play for the remaining of the game.

another awesome thing about duels, is that ties are broken by the order in which players "passed" first... you might not see how that is cool if you haven't played... but it really is soooo awesome... it's a "push your luck" scenario each round... and if someone runs to safety before you, they've got a really good chance of warding off an attack you send their way.  

I want to play again... it should be a snap to teach now that we know how to play...

currently HIGHLY RECOMENDED, as a strategy game, with real time randomness... that doesn't steal the spotlight

[2010/01/30]

quick disclaimer.. i played last night, and i think this game is making me delusional...

I still love playing this game, and i'd like to keep playing...  this time we played with 5 and that made it even more awesome than 3... 

heres where the delusion kicks in: i like this game so much i think i've convinced myself that this is a "Light game"... Make no mistake... this is a "Heavy game" with a natural feeling of randomness.. There is a crapload of math (that i don't mind at all).. but you should be aware of it before you play it or spring it on your friends


P.S. This is a deceptive statement I made about this game "It's only 4 turns, how long can it be"

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
規則:
"*每個玩家起始15元，1馬路，3牛仔，12地權板塊，顏色標記隨機放順位跟0分位置
*3/10元區放圈地，4/12元區放礦脈，剩下三格隨便抽，擲骰放一房子當市中心(周圍圍上馬路)，三槍放對應圖版位置，回合標記放1，在擲骰放九個山，逆順位開始順序放一次地權板塊，在正序放一次，並選取要使用的七個腳色放對應面
*遊戲進行四輪 每輪四個階段，最後記分
A 照目前順位每人選一個角色卡，選完照角色數字小到大排新順位
B 放牛仔 根據新順位輪流放一個，可放行動格，放土地來買地，攻擊防禦建築物，Pass並移順位軌到下面(下輪選角順序)
C 執行行動跟決鬥 照順序執行所有行動(牛仔放回供應區)，有兩人以上在同一格進行決鬥，擲骰加上手上槍數跟牛仔數，平手順位前的獲勝，贏的執行行動，輸的牛仔回手，建築物的話贏的可拿一半收入(向下取整)
*行動可不執行，但是牛仔一樣回供應區
>四元收入跟一格鐵路可放多個牛仔 不決鬥，放幾個執行幾次
>三槍該輪結束後要還
>買地 起始1元，九格內房子山脈建築都加1元，如果要決鬥，結算順序由第一個pass的決定
>建建築 根據規則建在自己的地權上，馬路相鄰只要對角即可，建築可以留著在之後的建築回合結束時蓋(如順序重要照跳過的順序)，蓋馬路不算行動
*每次件建築時一定要放一個房子，如果有房子周遭蓋滿(包括地權，河流，但版圖邊緣不算)，怎可改為升級該房子，房子可放在別人地權上但是要他同意，也可放空地，要馬路相連，不能蓋房子也不能蓋建築
*圈地跟礦脈不用蓋房子也不用跟馬路相連
>地權收入 場上每個地權兩元
>火力收入 手上的槍跟牛仔，每個兩元，三槍標記也算
>擲骰收入 丟兩顆骰多少拿多少
>收入結算 不用踩，所有玩家根據建築物拿收入，如果有決鬥結算完在拿，沒決鬥攻擊方直接拿 
*如果房子或山脈有特定玩家的地權，則只會給該玩家建築增益，如果成功踩在別人建築物上拿走一半收入(向下取整)
>拿分 兩個地權1分，兩個火力1分(向下取整)，建築物一分，買分
D 每輪結束 回合格移動，拿下回合牛仔，超過腳色上限的錢丟棄或10元換1分，建築物左移並補抽新的 
*遊戲結束 每個地權上建築物，山，房子兩分，腳色上限扣出後金錢，6元換1分，分數平手順位前的獲勝
***建築物  附近指九宮格
*房子 要接馬路，跟隨其他建築物蓋的
*圈地 隨便建，附近空格一格1元，加1槍
*藥房 要接馬路並蓋1房，附近房子跟擁有的圈地1個3元(圈地相鄰會算兩次)
*礦脈 隨便蓋 附近1山3元，加1槍
*銀行 要接馬路並蓋1房，附近房子跟擁有的礦脈1個3元
*沙龍  要接馬路並蓋1房，附近1房5元
*旅館  要接馬路並蓋1房，收入6元，本身算兩房
*教堂  要接馬路並蓋1房，直接相鄰的不能被搶
*監獄  要接馬路並蓋1房，兩槍
***腳色卡
*警長 白米寶的行動一定可做不能被決鬥，也不能挑起決鬥 變體:不能挑起決鬥只能防禦，每場輸了拿3分
*銀行家 9元 變體:錢換分
*雜貨員 8元或某類建築總收入翻倍 變體:每棟房子一分
*中國苦力 兩條馬路跟建築物半價 變體:5元買一棟蓋，建築物區馬上推左並補新的
*拓荒者 獲得一土地 變體:8元或1山1元
*隊長 花錢雇牛仔 變體:花錢買槍
*大盜 暫時加3槍 變體 暫時加2槍
***變體 ****
*決鬥變體 2人每人發六個決鬥板塊(0~5)跟15元， 3~5人，照人數那決鬥板塊洗勻每人發六個，起始錢為30-決鬥板塊合的金額，決鬥是不擲骰，改為使用板塊，使用完棄掉，全部用完從棄掉地方隨機抽一個
*河流變體 用河流版圖，只用六個山脈，河流不能建任何東西，礦脈相鄰河流也會加3元，兩條馬路(橋)才能過河，遊戲結束跟橋相鄰的建築3分不是2分
*同時行動變體 首位玩家放他的牛仔後，大家同時放牛仔到各區，可隨便移動，或放在拿，不想動就pass改順位，遊戲結束每個順位在他後面的玩家讓他獲得一分

RATING: N/A
1000 已拆未玩
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Falls into that middle ground where it's got elements that don't appeal to Eurogamers (eg the inevitable overt conflict) and those that don't appeal to Ameritrashers (eg optimisation of acquisition of VP's). The conflict is one strategic element to take into account - the investment in guns takes turns now (where you get nothing) in order to ensure you get what you want later. Those who don't invest in guns get their stuff now, but may lose expected actions later. Which way to go? For the peaceful minded, note who the next round's gunslingers are likely to be and make plans to do other things. One of the strengths is that there are many paths to follow so there are always options, but being prepared for multi-player luck to bear a fair proportion of the result will get you in the right mind-set to enjoy. And be psychologically prepared for the occasional really bad roll of the dice to kill your turn and lose you those last essential VPs that would have given you the game. With so few turns in the game to recover, a bad roll can dramatically affect your result. Everyone's got a sob story ... which is why I'm a bit equivocal on the game. I'd prefer a game to finish with emotional highs all round from a well fought game rather than downers on targeting, dice, etc.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
What I like:
- The spatial aspect makes it more interesting than many worker placement games
- Just 4 rounds, so it feels quick (our 2p learning game took 100 minutes, but felt shorter)
- The theme works well and fits the mechanics

What I don't like:
- There is quite a bit of conflict; I'm not sure if you could win playing defensively
- Each round requires a bit more planning than I would like, and the end-of-round money limit is probably the part that puts it over the line

I didn't expect to like CC, so it probably exceeded my expectations. It's clearly a very well-designed game, but hits a bit higher than I like both on weight (I'll say 3.0) and on conflict. Just not my thing.

RATING: 5
Each round is in two phases: placing workers and resolving workers and these two phases are separate. Placing the workers, where all the thinking/planning is done takes up a small proportion of the game compared to the "resolve workers" phase.  The resolve phase is burdened with a lot of fiddly bookkeeping.  It's occasionally livened up by the occasional duel, but duels are not that frequent once players realize they are not really all that beneficial, except, of course, in the final round.  As a result, I feel the game really drags. Another weakness is that whoever has the sheriff in the final round has an advantage that is very tough to overcome. The banker, perhaps, has the best shot at overcoming this advantage.   If I want a game where player's "shoot" each other, I will play something else.

RATING: 6
Everyone describes this game as "Caylus with guns"; I found the Caylus part to be enjoyable, but the guns, not so much.  Basically, anyone can challenge one of your actions, forcing both players to either simultaneously choose and flip a tile to see who wins in one variant, or roll dice in another.  Neither or these really appealed to me, and while it didn't happen too often, the possibility of it sapped a little of the fun out of the game.  The game did have an interesting spatial component, where players claimed spaces on a grid and built buildings there to generate income.  Overall though, this game felt too confrontational to me.

RATING: 8
Probably my favorite worker placement game.  I like how it has the role selection, limited rounds, tile laying/city building, and confrontation with its worker placement.  The confrontation aspect is special as if someone takes a spot you want, you can gunfight for them.  The loser will get a bonus for the next round, so not totally hosed over.

This game ends far too quickly, though, when everyone is wanting to get going with their engine.

RATING: 5
After the worker placement mechanic comes the variant 'worker displacement' in which contested hot spots are resolved with die rolls. Being the wild west setting, thematically it fits well, however emotionally I think it's a step back from the original mechanic.

Firstly, seeing the 'hot spots' isn't easy with the overwhelming choice of placements and fiddly rules of building conditions and their relevant powers. For me it delays the tension associated with a space, but a bigger problem is the fact that the majority of spaces aren't for sole possession dilutes the feeling of the tug of of too many things to do but not enough actions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a curious western themed worker placement game. The game features a method of action blocking where you can place your meeple in a spot someone else has claimed to have a duel with them right before resolution for the right to execute that spot. You can also place your meeples on other player's buildings to take half of that buildings production until they duel you off. The duel consists of adding up unspent guys plus gun tokens and then rolling a D6. This can feel a little random at times, but it's mitigatable randomness by focusing on getting more gun tokens. The loser of a duel gets the worker back to place again in the next round, whereas normally spent workers are lost. The worker being returned to the player, also means they get 1 extra firepower if they have another duel that round.

I like a lot about this game. I love how the city expands. I like that when you build most buildings you need to place a red house token somewhere legal for it to be, which both serves to fill up the map awkwardly, but also gives players big scoring opportunities when they place buildings that want to be adjacent to houses. I like the mitigatable action blocking, although the D6 thing is a little troublesome. I also like how players can sort of hinder the leader by blocking their scoring action or by taking some of their money for themselves. Here are my problems with the game...

With 3 players the game is too loose. The board grows too slowly and the action spaces feel empty. With 6 players the game is far far too tight, with few to no actions you can take other than start a duel if you aren't going very early in turn order. The Train station building from the expansion seems to be worth way too many points.  I'm not sure why the job that gives you the unduelable worker goes first (or second if playing with the 0 guy) in turn order. This seems like a doubly strong ability, as you can't place the unduelable worker in a space to start a duel. The iconography on the building tiles is pretty poor. The buildings require you to read a passage in the rulebook every time a new type of one flips, and they can be hard to remember in the face of remembering all the other rules. In the second expansion they use one of the same icons from the base game to have a different meaning, which is really troublesome.

The biggest problem with the game is the endgame scoring. Throughout the game there are action spots available that let you covert dollars into points at different ratios. In each of the 4 rounds, the next best conversion spot disappears.  Based on the character ability tile you select, you have a different ability to hold money between rounds. Any money you can't hold and didn't spend gets turned into points at a 10:1 rate, as opposed to 2/3/4/5:1 rate. All of these conversion spots are duel-able, which means taking the spot doesn't mean that you'll necessarily get to resolve it. The only sure way to convert dollars to points is at the end of the game, you discard down to your dollars held limit at that 10:1 rate, and then you can convert the remaining money at 6:1, which isn't bad, but it is bad when you look at how different the money held skills are. Several guys in the base setup only can hold $20, whereas one can hold over $100. For me, this makes scoring a frustrating experience.

:nostar: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with different player counts
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
2013-12-07: I always remember whether or not I played a game. Not this time. All my comments still apply, even the snide comment about stacking cowboy meeples.

2011-04-28: Yet another worker placement game.

Bonus: the cowboy meeples are fun to stack!

To be fair, here's what makes Carson City different from other worker placement games:

* No ubiquitous cubes serving as resources. Instead, they are replaced with land plots, buildings, money, and guns. all of which serve a unique purpose in the game. There's no "give me two blues, a red, and a yellow" in this game.

* The ability to invest in another resource -- guns -- to steal a chosen role away from someone else.

Still, it's a worker placement game, one of my least favourite of gaming genres, and Carson City is no exception when it comes to the genre's shortcomings:

* Poor complexity of rules to depth ratio. Because of the nature of worker placement games, in which every role must be unique, rules tend to be very long and must be understood by the players in their entirety. Carson City has actually two worker placement stages, which adds to the complexity of the rules. Still, I don't find the game particularly strategically deep, but I do think it's quite tactical (see the next point)

* I find most Euros much more tactical than strategic, and worker placement games are no exception. Players mention that "good Euros" have multiple paths of winning. However, there are usually a limited number of paths, and there is usually no way to "branch" off these paths to pursue more creative ways of winning.

RATING: 9
Component Quality:
Excellent quality thick board used for the majority of pieces. The parcel tiles are thin, but work best that way. I especially enjoy the artwork on the role cards.

Gameplay:
My all-time favorite game. All the mechanics work together so smoothly. It's competitive, but not in a really nasty way. Love the old west theme and the opportunity for players who are trailing behind to duel for a chance to catch up a little. It's also nice that there are variants in the game for more expert play styles and less chancey dice-focused games. I've heard the complaint that there's too much micro-management in keeping track of the property incomes, but I enjoy flipping all of the tiles 

Overall: This is my perfect game. What others see as flaws I see as fun. I will always be willing to play Carson City.

Also, the expansion is awesome if you can get your hands on it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've heard this game called "Caylus with guns". While Caylus is a much deeper game, that phrase does a pretty good job of describing Carson City. I particularly like the city building aspect of the game. Not only do you have to choose what actions to take, but you need to pay attention to how Carson City is developing and what that means for the income of your buildings. The interaction in the game is terrific and the thematic integration is very strong. For me, however, the game loses points for the rulebook. Parts of the rulebook are subject to varying interpretations, and there are lots of little rules that are easy to miss. Having played this game twice now, I'm still finding that there are rules that we've misplayed. Looking forward to playing this one again.

RATING: 6
Interesting game. Over four rounds players develop the town of Carson claiming plots of land. The objective is to finish with the highest victory points - you can do this in various way, buildings are worth points at the end and during the game you can exchange money or score points for plots, buildings, and guns. This is worker placement but there is an interesting gunfight mechanism to determine who takes a particular action in  a conflict.
My appreciation was slightly marred because in my first game I won by well over 20 points, the game seems hugely dependent on initial position and opening moves.

Carson City: Gold & Guns Expansion
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
22-02-2015. Rated after 1 play. I was trying to hunt a copy of Carson City because I was curious. I am sad to say I didn't like the game at all!

In Carson City you are trying to build a city. Each round you select a role, that gives you a special ability but also limits the amount of money you can keep from round to round. Each round, you get a number of workers that you send out one-by-one following the other players turns around an action track that is resolved in order. Many of the actions allow you to get resources (guns, money, plots), build buildings on your plots (that produce money dependent on (neighboring) tiles in the city. Then, your buildings produce money and there are actions where you get VPs. The interesting twist in the game is the competition, if you place our worker at the same action as another worker you fight. You also can start a fight when you place your worker at the building of another player. When resolving the action space, both players roll a die, add the amount of guns you have and the loser gets their meeple returned for next round and the winner takes the action. In case the meeple was attacking a building, you get half the income from the building.

I didn't really like Carson City, mainly because of one choice that happened during the game. First off, I don't like that your workers can get shot. That REALLY can screw over your plans. In addition, I didn't like one of the roles (Sheriff), which who gets an additional meeple that can't be shot. As you can convert your income into VPs for 10 (or 5 in the last round if you take the action), the game becomes scripted if the Sheriff takes the action, all people start beating on the Sheriffs income while they can't score points themselves anymore. I DO like interaction in games, but here it felt random and forced you into scripted actions, which made an otherwise enjoyable game dud completely for me.

RATING: 7
Disappointing - the direct conflict, and luck of the dice were unappealing to me in a game of this type. I think the combat could have been done much better. (I understand there are variants to address this - I'd like to check them out) - I found the game tantalisingly close to be being very good apart from the luck element which virtually ruined it for me (and I won so this is not sour grapes!) 

There's been a lot of comparison to Caylus, and the similarities are clear - place all workers, then activate them, moving down a road. The "screw you" element in Caylus though, the movement of the Provost, is infinitely more elegant, than the lucky dice roll of this game. Surely this issue has been addressed in some variants? The rest of it is interesting - the characters and buildings - and the interaction of the buildings is cool - although somewhat unthematic - for example - a drugstore will surely benefit from ALL houses around it regardless of which player built them? This would make interaction between buildings even more interesting. Anyway - this is close to being a very good game but for the lazy design decision to insert dice combat, IMHO.

Érdeklődéssel ültem le a Carson City-hez. Korábban már hallottam az egész szabályát, az alapján elég bonyolultnak találtam és tényleg nem könnyű első játékra mindent átlátni. 5 játékos esetén már szerintem elég nehéz minden épület bevételét jól beállítani, figyelni kell ne essen ebben hiba. A játék amúgy egy Caylus, Citadella és Tigris keveréke elsőre, másodikra se sokkal több csak egy kis konfliktustörténettel, amiben viszont alapesetben szerepet játszik a kocka. A vadnyugati hangulathoz hozzá tartozik, de sosem szeretem ha egy komoly játékot kockával bolondítanak meg, így ebben sem tetszett ez. Szerencsére van kocka nélküli variáns is a szabályban, de maga a párbajkonfliktus sem hiányozna alapvetően számomra a játékból. Tehát a társas már jól bevált elemekből építkezik. Szerepválasztás, akciómezőválasztás és lapkalerakás. Semmi újdonságot nem lehet felfedezni benne, de nem rossz az összegyúrás. Nálam tipikus 6-os játék. Semmi extra, egy kevésbé tetsző résszel erős 6-os.

[2012-11-10]
A második játékra romlott a kép. Nagyon macera figyelni az egyes épületek helyes bevételét, a kocka nagyon nagy szerencseszerepet visz a játékba. Túl sokat markoltak a készítők, talán a kevesebb több lett volna. A kocka nélküli változatnak még adnom kellene egy esélyt, de nem valószínű, hogy fogok, akkor ha esetleg mégis összejönne lehet emelek majd egyet a most lerontott ponton.

RATING: 8

All in all a great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Carson City stands out from the majority of worker placement games, as it's one of the few without yet another resource management mechanic (of which I personally grew a bit tired after Agricola, Caylus, Stone Age et cetera). Instead, this game has a strong spatial element and introduces direct conflict. Players are building the town of Carson City and contest for the best parcels and the best buildings. A role selection mechanic gives players certain benefits, opening a ton of different strategies. The worker placement mechanic feels fresh, since it adds a new kind of turn angst. Because more than one player can select the same action a duel has to be fought to decide who can perform said action. There are ways to migigate this, but this will cost you extra -valueable- actions. The variant where duels are fought with the blind bidding tiles is highly recommended for a more strategic experience.
Carson City is a game with many layered subleties, like the dual turn order mechanics and the cash limits associated with each role. Since the game lasts only four rounds and actions are really scarce, each decision is impotant and meaningful. These two factors make Carson City a very strategic game.

Gold & Guns is a great expansion as it adds more roles, and you now play the game with a random mix of 7 roles. This adds a lot of replayability and strategic depth as new scoring paths open up and change every game.

Owned expansions: Gold & Guns, A New Beginning.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This game is pretty thematic, and has a nice Wild West theme built into it. I like the way in which the action track resolves in order, like Caylus, and I enjoy the different kinds of buildings and how they work together and affect the board. The dueling can seem a bit random, and too much seems to be won or loss on an individual die roll, and we should play with a variant to reduce the luck in the future.

RATING: 7
1 play, 4er

An interesting role-selection, worker-placement, economic, tile-laying/controlling, city-building (buildings come out in random order) game with a western theme. Worker placement is a bit modified, as cowboys can duel over the same action! Unfortunately, the basic dueling rules (add your gun strength to a d6) seem to be overly random, and the variant (using tiles 0-5, that are all mixed and dealt randomly!) doesn't seem to be much better! Why not just give everyone their 0-5 and play with those?!

Pretty enjoyable, even with the basic rules. Looking forward to trying the advanced role tiles, and better dueling rules!

Oh, good production value except for one thing: who the hell cuts/folds a board like that?!?

RATING: 6.5
Carson City has an interesting take on worker placement.  Namely, you get to duel other players for spots on the worker placement track.  This creates some fun interaction; but ultimately it adds an unplanned wrench in the works to an otherwise well oiled game.  The fighting for spots can be a kingmaker type of move.  As with many multiplayer conflict games, the looser of a fight, can quickly fall behind, not something that modern euro's take kindly too.  Don't get me wrong, I love me some conflict, but in a seemingly medium weight worker placed net euro, it just doesn't fit.  It boils down to this, if I am playing a euro, and I get crushed, it should be because I made bad decisions, here, I can get crushed if another player deems it so.  I do want ot play it again to see if my initial assesment is accurate.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 2
Fundamentally the game is a very poor design which is close enough to being broken amongst competitive players as to make it no fun at all.  The problem stems from the gunfight aspect.  Because of the accumulative nature of the revolvers, the design forces players to literally only collect guns and/cowboys for the first two turns of the four turn game, and further when the prisons come out.  If anyone is given the chance to collect a majority in revolvers they are unstoppable and everyone must play for second place--while its possible for another player to jump on the grenade to stop a gun heavy player, it just means neither will win and one of the other players will take the top spot.  An additional problems lies in the fact that there are far too any decision points when placing the cowboys and the game takes an inordinate amount of time--sure it could be played in 90 minutes but if you want to play well it'll take at least 2.5 hours and probably 3.  The game is nowhere near good enough to justify that amount of time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Carson City is Caylus with violence: a shorter, more brutal re-implementation of the road system. It isn't "worker placement"; it's "gunfighter placement" -- if another player takes a space you want, you can fight them for it. Buildings aren't placed on the road, but on a grid. You can rob directly rob another player's building of income, or build around it to make that income go away. It's incredibly cutthroat.

I was thrilled to finally play this game and expected to love it. I want to give it another try because I can't believe how much I hated that first play. There was nothing in particular -- I even didn't mind the dice.

My problem, I think, is the *kind* of decisions it forces: in most of the games I love, I feel like I'm trying to choose the best of many good possibilities. So many opportunities, so little time! In Carson City I felt like I was choosing the least worst.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
An 'earn cash, buy VP' worker-placement game, but with the fundamental defect of rewarding bullies rather than planners: plan what you like, but if you lack fire power you'll lose a lot. Thematic? I think not - in the Wild West if I had lots of cash I'm pretty sure I could go hire some hot-shots to drive the bullies away. Not in this game, I can’t!
 
I've tried both the basic duelling rules and the 'might is right' variant, and neither works for me. Players with more fire-power tend to win duels and get the chance to acquire yet more fire-power. There is no compensation for losing; no alternate (non-competitive) way to get more fire-power - like simply buying in hired guns.

RATING: 8
Carson City is an enjoyable worker placement game with plenty of options to increase your scoring.  It's short so this must be taken into consideration from the start.  The first turn feels a little empty but then it accelerates so quickly into the plan.  The decision of which character to take is fun and helps you out of situations where the current plan has faltered a bit. 

I have not played enough to discover variable strategies other than fight for the right to get the cheap victory point spot.  I want to believe there is a big money strat that allows you to ignore the vp purchases and concentrate on other things like major property incomes.  We will see.

I like the duels.  It makes it different and is something more like a cold war.  The revolvers just seem to stack up by game end and can be a viable point grab.  In a 4 player game fighting is a must so they become very important.

The buildings combos and alternate point bursts remind me of Cuba.  This one is way more fun and flows much better without unnecessary cube converting and such.

Thoughts on 2p : Must be played on the tighter Carson River side of the board.  It seems getting in the face of your opponent is a must to create tension.  If players decide to do their own thing then the game can feel mechanical and missing something.  This makes me see the beauty of Agricola 2 player.

4p : We felt the sheriff was too powerful on the yellow side and will be using the red side for future games.  I had the task of calculating incomes and checking for income changes.  It's the fiddliest part of the game but the coolest. It was a little tough with 2 glasses of wine but not terribly so.  

Must remember to pass!

Really needs more than 2 players unless those two players really want to get down and dirty with stealin' & shootin'.

Also Expansion : The Indian, Gold & Guns

Fun factor : :star::star::star::star::nostar:

Fulfillment Factor : :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:

RATING: 6.4
I was a little underwhelmed with this. It promised so much and was only ... decent.

Update: It's actually a pretty clever WP game that plays in a sensible amount of time (and seems faster than it is). The downside being that the dueling mechanism which makes the game stand out can also result in players being shut out for a whole round or more which is essentially devastating.

RATING: 4
unfortunately we just got 1/2 a game in
-------

Full game played now. Unfortunately its most unique feature is also its biggest weakness. When I heard of this confrontational worker placement I thought it's a good idea to alter your regular concept of workerplacement which goes "Oh! So you want the carrots? Well, you've been here first. Go ahead. I'll take some wheat instead" into something quite the opposite, but unfortunately the game is just too confrontational and in desperate need of balancing. Once a player falls behind with the pistols the player(s) with more guns can bully him around. In terms of theme it might be quite coherent to have the way of the gun, but when you cannot make any decisions but your opponents are playing you, then there's something seriously wrong. And once you're behind with guns it's in the hands or rather the good will of your opponent(s) wether they will or will not allow you to catch up. Also, I have a strong feeling that the game is scripted in a way that the amount of duels will skyrocket during round 4 of every single game, because everyone wants to use the last possibility to score and as many of those last possibilities as possible. Endgame score was 31-31-18, but could have easily been 31(31)-28(18)-25(31).

RATING: 4
Worker placement game that also has a little bit of role selection, which I like.

However, the game, especially with two players, seems a bit short. There are only four rounds, and with two players they go by very quickly. Also, it can be hard to remember all of the rules for each building. Some of them require you to build a house onto the board, some of them have to be placed next to a road, some of them can be placed anywhere. I've made myself a little cheatsheet to help with this and it makes the game flow smoother.

There's also a broken strategy that involves taking the character that gives you guns then taking the space that gives you guns. I've read online that playing with that set of characters (which is the set that the rules recommend using) does have balance problems and you should use the alternative characters. That's not very promising.

Sold.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I can't see this one climbing past the 8.5 I currently give it, but it is currently a favorite.

First off, I like the theme. Western themes are still bit underrepresented in my opinion.

Second, superficially one has a LOT of choices, but one can smartly whittle them down to a few best ones. And if you can't, purchase the ability to get more (if you have the opportunity to select the correct personality that is). And if you don't have a lot of good choices, save your selections for next year.

You see, the point is one not only has a lot of choices, but, more importantly, one has a lot of flexibility. Planning is essential and the decision tree is fairly large, BUT, and this is also important, it is not unwieldy. Something I find frustrating in a game like Dominant Species.

I tend to like tactical games and playing from the gut a bit more than games with broad strategic horizons and perfect information. Carson City is a good compromise. A strategy is important and there are many to employ, but I am not at all overwhelmed. 

Another way to put it is that I can always look back on my games and immediately see the large and small mistakes I made. I appreciate that.

I also love the inclusion of duels. It helps the theme, presents a nice twist action drafting, adds some depth to decision making and keeps things lively. It was a great design decision to allow the loser to get their cowboy back. If one lost their cowboy I think the game might be driven too much in the direction of guns and saving cowboys. Choices would narrow.

Two complaints: 
One, is that it takes quite awhile to explain. I prefer to get people playing and explain the game as we go, but one has to explain all the spaces on the action track and their context. Consequently one has to explain nearly all of the rules before starting. 

Two, there are a lot of rules. Little rules and things you have to remember. It all means that there is a larger barrier to entry than I generally prefer in a game. It is too bad because I'd love to share this game with more people.




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 4
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 2


Overall - 1

RATING: 9
Carson City is a strategic game. The game is played in 4 rounds and in each one of them the players choose a character (there are 7 available) that gives certain advantages, after that cowboys are placed on action fields on the board to build Carson city. Players can claim ground, erect special buildings, houses or construct roads. When there are several players on 1 action field, a duel is fought. During play, money (to construct) & points can be earned. The player who earns most points wins the game.

RATING: 4
Some decent subsystems, but the whole is less than the sum of its parts. There are too many actions which are too similar, so whether or not you duel with another player is often quite arbitrary - and losing a duel is significant. The fact that only your buildings and neutral houses work with your buildings mean that there is little interest in parcel selection - basically everyone wants a big block. Etc.

The basic duel rules are terrible - having a small number of very significant die rolls is not good design. In the second game we used the tiles variant, which is better but also not great, as a very low or a very high total is bad (the latter due to the low cash). 

I suspect that the alternate character powers are better than the basic ones, so it is possible that with enough official and unofficial variants (e.g., give out fixed sets of duel tiles) a better game could emerge, but I should not have to figure this out. If there is a best way to play the game, THAT should be the basic game.

RATING: 4
A high conflict Eurogame that is not in my taste. Technically speaking, there is nothing wrong with the game per se. This is a standard Euro game of worker placement and role selection with a huge dose of combat to antagonize each other. There appears to be multiple strategies to pick up a few victory points here and there, but one has to quickly adapt to strategies adopted by others. If one player in the group goes marauding and picks up a trove of reckless cowboys to terrorize the town, then it matters not if you are the richest or most influential person in town. You will have to constantly protect yourself by arming your own group of thugs to prevent constant pillaging. If you play this game like any engine building game to generate wealth and points, you will likely lose in a frustrating manner as your position will be constantly targeted by duels. In our games, play revolves around the duels way too much to be entertaining. There is constant die rolling to resolve duels with some players completely dominating the board with multiple guns. Not my cup of tea. Too much of a dice-fest even though I love player interactions.

RATING: 8
Rating based on one play.  The best worker placement game I have ever contemplated.  A fun fun game with a brain burning depth..... So many  choices! 

Players take turns putting down their little cowboy folk.  You can squat on plots of land, and then build businesses on them (saloons, banks, ranches, whore houses (kidding)).  They earn you money. But you have a ton of other choices too, such as earn a victory point for every two plots you have, buy a victory point for 3 dollars a point, get three roads, get three guns, etc.  All of these things you get by placing one of your little cowboys on a square that indicates that action. But you wait until everyone has place their cowboys before you perform the action.  If two cowboys are on the same square....a duel...and one remains.

Will play more........want to right now.

RATING: 5.5
Decisions are generally fairly tough, but there's a handful of things I'm not fond of.  The sheer randomness of duels is probably my biggest complaint; this is somewhat offset in the earlier rounds as you don't lose the action entirely, but it is crushing in the final rounds.

Tracking your income each round is pretty fiddly as it changes round to round.  Aspects of the game feel awkwardly tacked on, such as the saved money cap.  The variant roles also seem questionably balanced, with the Coolie being ridiculously strong compared to the others.

Overall if you're actively interested in a worker placement game with a lot of dueling randomness it's probably worth checking out, but isn't the game for me.

RATING: N/A
Carson City is a rootin' tootin' game of cowboys 'n guns - lots o' guns.
\13\\13\
There are 4 rounds per game. At the beginning of each round, players choose a character (there are 7 available) that gives them certain advantages (for example the sheriff prevents any duels from happening wherever he is placed).
\13\\13\
After selecting characters, your cowboys are placed on action track locations that allow you to construct buildings, houses or roads; claim ground; earn money; or score victory points. When more than one player is on the same location, get ready, it is time for a duel! Roll the dice and see if you are the last one standing and lay claim to the goods!
\13\\13\
During the game, you can take various actions that earn you victory points for your plots, pistols (the hired help) and buildings. At the end of the game, your buildings, houses, mountains and money contribute to your victory points and the person with the most points wins. So go round up your posse of gunslingers and get ready for some Wild West action in Carson City!

RATING: 8
1 game with 2; 1 game with 3. Carson City is a conversion engine with worker placement and role selection, which are not my preferred mechanics.  However, CC allows workers to be placed on the same action, and a "shoot out" decides who can take the action.  This added dimension of direct conflict makes CC very interesting and a lot of fun for me.  This is one of my favorite worker placement games.  Works better with more players.  I think 3 works well, and 4 may be best.  I think 5 would bog the game down.

RATING: 5
I thought I might like Carson City, because it's got a tang to it.  Dice battles determine who gets to use each worker space, and there's a spatial element to town construction and the generation of income.

But as the scene ends and we ride off into the sunset, it's still the same old worker placement mechanic. I'll never, ever play another WP game again, and this time I really mean it.  Though when you're surrounded, and low on ammo, it's tough to fight them off.

[i]Update:[/i]

I've decided to kill myself rather than play another worker placement game.  Hence the epitaph on my Boot Hill tombstone:

[i]A worker has been placed for the last time. There shall be no other.  RIP.[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
High risk, high payoff, highly chaotic worker placement game in the same vein as Pillars of the Earth, Stone Age, and Vasco de Gama. Players receive most of their victory points from relatively few actions, with certain actions resulting in 20-30% of a player's final score, yet another player can snuff out the opportunity with a single duel. The randomness in Carson City is situational, often manifesting in situations like needing the same buildings as the Mercenary (a losing proposition, to say the least), getting picked on for winning because players can directly shaft the leader by placing on their building, or arbitrarily avoiding contention because you're the only guy who wants a particular type of building. Some things that should probably happen every game:

1st round Sheriff on the cheapest Mine/Ranch
3rd round Sheriff on $4 -> VP, ~10-15 VP's
3rd round Banker, stockpile to $120, then choose Sheriff 4th round for $5 -> VP
4th round Banker to accumulate up to $120, = 20 VP's

Four starting strategies appear automatic:

Sheriff - Cheapest Mine/Ranch
Coolie - Most expensive buildings (uncontested)
Captain - Three extra cheap tracts, $2 per tract, middle priced building
Mercenary - Cheapest buildings not Sheriff'ed, guaranteed.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I enjoy the twist in this game of dueling with other players and being able to place your workers on spots that other players have placed their workers. Taking this risk adds for a fun random element in the game that can be mitigated by going for gun tokens which add to your dice rolls.  Though the game does have some randomness, it is not overly random by any means and is still a Euro at hear.  To add to that, I feel like the dice rolling fits the old west theme of the game and suits it perfectly.  To put it simply, this is one of the best thematic euros you will ever play and is one that will never leave my collection.  Its a simply awesome game!   It is so much fun to build your own part of the city out west and see the entire board be built by yourself and others.  The buildings that you build serve to gain you money or other special abilities but the fear of another player playing on your building to take your income is constant.  I enjoy the snaking action spots at the top of the board, where you put your workers.  Those are laid out nicely.  One of the best aspects of the game is getting to choose a special character every round that gives you some type of in game bonus and the ability to carry over a certain amount of money in the game.  You can go for the one you want or go for one that you know your opponent desperately needs.  This is an extremely awesome game experience!  Just go get it!

Note: Plano Box

Note 2: Note: Includes a custom Artscow bag to hold building tiles.
